Nikah — Marriage

Matters Where Oath is Not Administered: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath administration in nine matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine matters where Abu Hanifa does not administer an oath, explain the reasoning based on nukul as transfer, distinguish the Sahibayn's view, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Nukul is a transfer, not admission; where transfer is invalid, oath is not administered — except in property.

In Hanafi law, oath plays a crucial role in resolving disputes. But the Imam disagrees with his students on whether the defendant should be made to swear in certain cases like marriage, lineage, and hudud. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the dispute and its practical implications for judges and litigants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Nine Matters
List the nine matters where Abu Hanifa says no oath is administered: nikah, raj'a, fay', istilad, riqq, nasab, wala', hadd, li'an.
Note the exception for marriage involving wealth.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's Reasoning
Abu Hanifa treats nukul (refusal to swear) as a transfer (badal) that cuts off dispute without accusing the defendant of lying. These matters do not permit transfer, so judgment by nukul is invalid, analogous to qisas for life.
The analogy contrasts with property, where transfer is allowed.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's View
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad treat nukul as admission (iqrar). Since admission is valid in all claims, oath should be administered, and judgment can be based on nukul.
The other three schools also follow this view.
4
Note the Exception for Wealth
If the claim involves wealth (e.g., dower in marriage), oath is administered by consensus. This shows the dispute is limited to non-wealth aspects.
5
Recognize the Mufta Bihi
The text states 'wa bihi yufta' — the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is followed in fatwa. Thus, in practice, oath is administered in these matters.
This reflects the later Hanafi reliance on the Sahibayn's opinion.

Source Text

(عيني، فتح) ورق بأن ادعى على مجهول النسب أنه عبده وهو يجحد أو بالعكس. (عيني) ونسب: بأن ادعى على مجهول النسب أنه ابنه وأنكر الآخر، أو بالعكس. (عيني) وولاء : بأن ادعى على معروف النسب أنه معتقه وأنكر الآخر، أو بالعكس، أو كان ذلك في ولاء الموالاة. (عيني) وحد ولعان أي ولا يستحلف في حد بأن ادعى على آخر أنك قد قذفتني بالزنا وعليك الحد، وهو ينكر ، وكذا لا يستحلف في لعان بأن ادعت امرأة على زوجها أنه قذفها بالزنا، وعليه اللعان، وهو منكر، ففي الصورتين لا يستحلف إجماعاً إلا إذا تضمن حقاً بأن علق عتق عبده بالزنا - أي زنا نفسه - وقال: إن زنيت فأنت حر، فادعى العبد أنه قد زنى ولا بينة للعبد عليه، استحلف المولى، حتى إذا نكل يثبت العتق دون الزنا، وإنما لا يستحلف في الحد؛ لأنه يندرئ بالشبهات وفي اللعان؛ لأنه في معنى الحد، وقالوا: إنه يستحلف في التعزير؛ لأنه في حكم الأموال، ولذلك يصح فيه العفو والإبراء. (عيني، فتح)
Book of Claims, 9, Rulings of Claims. (Perhaps a page number). And he is not made to swear in marriage, return to wife, reconciliation, concubinage, slavery, lineage, patronage, fixed punishment, and mutual imprecation. Meaning the defendant in a marriage devoid of wealth according to Abu Hanifa. But if the woman claims that he married her for such-and-such, or claims maintenance and the husband denies, then he is made to swear. And return (raj'a) after the waiting period, for if he claims return during the waiting period, it is established by his statement even if she denies him; because he claims a matter that he can initiate at present. And reconciliation (fay'): meaning reconciliation of divorce by fault after the expiration of its period; and if he claims reconciliation during the period of divorce by fault, it is established by his statement. And concubinage (istilad): by a slave-girl claiming to her master that she gave birth to this child from him, or a child that died, and the master denies; and it is not possible from the other side; for if the master claimed, concubinage would be established by his admission and her denial is not considered. Similarly, fixed punishment and mutual imprecation, unlike the rest of the mentioned things; for in them, claim from both sides is possible, while in fixed punishment and mutual imprecation, the plaintiff can only be the one who has been slandered. And all this according to Abu Hanifa. And the reason he does not require an oath in the six matters is because nukul is a transfer and permission, and the transfer cuts off the dispute by paying what the opponent claims; and it is said: leaving off prevention, i.e., dispute, and turning away from it. For if we interpret nukul as admission, we would be accusing him of lying in his denial; but if we make it a transfer, the dispute is cut off without accusation, and this is more appropriate to protect a Muslim from being suspected of lying. And these are rights in which transfer does not apply, so no judgment is passed based on nukul in them, like retribution for life, unlike property. The gist is that every matter that permits permission by consent initially is judged based on his nukul, and what does not, not. It cannot be said that Abu Hanifa left the well-known hadith, which is his saying 'The oath is upon the one who denies' by opinion, and that is not permissible; because he did not negate the obligation of the oath in them, but he says: since the oath does not achieve its benefit (judgment based on nukul) because it is a transfer that does not apply in them, so it falls like the fall of obligation upon a person with an excuse from whom the performance of prayer is not realized due to the loss of purpose. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is made to swear in them; because nukul is like admission, and judgment based on admission is permissible, so too based on nukul as in property. And the three (i.e., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say this (the two's view?) and according to it fatwa is given. And it should be said here: 'in marriage devoid of wealth' meaning when the purpose is not wealth. But if the purpose is wealth, like if a woman claims that a man married her for a thousand dirhams and divorced her before consummation, and she has half the dowry, then he is made to swear by consensus; if he refuses, judgment is passed against him for half the dowry. (Ayni, Fath) And slavery (riqq): if he claims on a person of unknown lineage that he is his slave and he denies, or vice versa. (Ayni) And lineage (nasab): if he claims on a person of unknown lineage that he is his son and the other denies, or vice versa. (Ayni) And patronage (wala'): if he claims on a person of known lineage that he is his freed slave and the other denies, or vice versa, or if it is in patronage of allegiance. (Ayni) And fixed punishment (hadd) and mutual imprecation (li'an): meaning he is not made to swear in a fixed punishment case if he claims that another person slandered him with fornication and that the hadd applies, and he denies; likewise, he is not made to swear in mutual imprecation if a woman claims that her husband slandered her with fornication and that li'an applies, and he denies. In both cases, he is not made to swear by consensus, unless it includes a right, such as if he suspended the emancipation of his slave on fornication—i.e., his own fornication—and said: 'If I commit fornication, you are free,' and the slave claims that he did commit fornication with no evidence for the slave, then the master is made to swear; and if he refuses, emancipation is established without the fornication. And he is not made to swear in fixed punishment because it is repelled by doubts, and in mutual imprecation because it is in the sense of fixed punishment. And they said: he is made to swear in discretionary punishment (ta'zir) because it is in the category of property, and therefore forgiveness and waiver are valid in it. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage lists nine matters in which the defendant is not required to take an oath according to Abu Hanifa: marriage (when devoid of wealth), return to wife (raj'a), reconciliation (fay'), concubinage (istilad), slavery (riqq), lineage (nasab), patronage (wala'), fixed punishment (hadd), and mutual imprecation (li'an). The reasoning is that nukul is considered a transfer (badal) which cuts off the dispute without accusing the defendant of lying. However, these matters are not subject to transfer like property, so judgment cannot be based on nukul. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, holding that nukul is like admission, so the defendant should be made to swear; their view is the basis for fatwa. The passage also explains exceptions: if the matter involves wealth (e.g., a marriage with a dower), then the oath is administered by consensus. Similarly, in hadd and li'an, the oath is not administered because they are repelled by doubts, but if a right is attached (like emancipation), the oath is taken.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): The defendant's refusal to take a sworn oath in court, which can lead to judgment against him.
البذل (al-badhal): Transfer or payment; the idea that nukul cuts off the dispute by 'paying' the claim.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): Return to the wife during the waiting period after a revocable divorce.
الفيء (al-fay'): Reconciliation by a husband who has sworn an oath of abstention (ila') before the period expires.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): A master's concubinage with a slave-girl resulting in a child, giving her the status of umm walad.
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law, e.g., for slander (qadhf).
اللعان (al-li'an): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view adopted for fatwa in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on administering an oath to the defendant in a claim of marriage (without dower)?
A) The oath is not administered because marriage is not a property matter.
B) The oath is administered because nukul is like admission.
C) The oath is only administered if the claim involves wealth.
D) The oath is administered only if the defendant consents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, oath is administered even in non-property matters, as per the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because nukul functions like admission.

Oath of Husband in Divorce Claims Before Consummation
Ruling on administering oath to husband when wife claims divorce before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on administering an oath to the husband in divorce claims before consummation and explain the consequence of his refusal.

Core Idea
If the wife claims divorce before consummation, the husband must swear; if he refuses, he pays half the dower.

When a wife claims that her husband divorced her before consummation, the husband is required to take an oath. This ruling is based on consensus because the claim involves a financial element—the dower. If the husband refuses to swear (nukul), he becomes liable for half the dower. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the conditions under which the oath is administered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim scenario
The scenario is where the wife claims that the husband divorced her before consummation. Note that the same rule applies to all divorce claims, but the text focuses on this scenario for clarity.
The restriction to 'before consummation' is just for illustration; the oath is administered in any divorce claim.
2
State the ruling on the oath
The husband is made to swear an oath (yastahlif al-zawj). This is wajib (obligatory) by consensus.
The judicial authority administers the oath.
3
State the consequence of refusal (nukul)
If the husband refuses to take the oath, he must pay half the dower (which is the amount due in a divorce before consummation).
Nukul establishes financial liability but does not prove the divorce itself.
4
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is twofold: (1) the claim involves a financial element (mahr or nafaqah), and (2) there is consensus (ijma') that the oath is administered in divorce cases.
The text explicitly says 'because it is a claim of property' and 'by consensus'.
5
Distinguish financial from personal status
The nukul only establishes the financial obligation (half the dower). It does not prove the occurrence of divorce or affect the marriage's validity.
The marriage is not established by nukul; only the financial claim is proven.

Source Text

And the husband (raised) as a conjunction to the thief: i.e., the husband is also made to swear an oath. When the woman claims: and there is no difference whether it is the dower or the maintenance of the 'iddah, and the restriction to before consummation is agreed upon, but the benefit of specifying the scenario is in divorce before consummation: it is that the claim of dower does not differ whether the claim is for the full dower or half, and whether it is a claim of dower in the case of divorce or not; and the administration of the oath applies in divorce by consensus, and likewise in marriage if she claims the sadaq or maintenance, because it is a claim of property; then the property is established by his nukul, but the marriage is not established. That she claims divorce before consummation, then if he refrains, he is liable for half the dower before consummation because she says: 'You divorced me before you entered upon me' and this is by consensus because it involves a claim of property; and the restriction to 'before consummation' is agreed upon because the administration of the oath applies in divorce absolutely by consensus. He is liable for half the dower because it is established by divorce before consummation.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a wife claims that her husband divorced her before consummation (i.e., before the marriage was consummated). In such a claim, the husband is made to swear an oath. If he refuses (nukul), he becomes liable to pay half the dower. The reasoning is that the claim involves a financial element (the dower), and nukul can establish financial obligations. The text notes that there is consensus (ijma') on administering the oath in divorce claims because they involve property. The restriction to 'before consummation' is only for illustration; in absolute terms, the oath is administered in all divorce claims. The passage also clarifies that the wife's claim could be for the full dower or half, depending on the type of divorce, but the oath is administered regardless. If the husband refuses to swear, he must pay half the dower (which is the amount due in a divorce before consummation). The marriage itself is not established by nukul; only the financial liability is.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower or bridal gift that the husband must pay to the wife.
الطلاق قبل الوطء (al-talaq qabl al-wat'): Divorce pronounced by the husband before he has consummated the marriage.
النكول (al-nukul): The refusal of a party to take the oath in a judicial proceeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In divorce claims involving property, the husband must swear; refusal establishes financial liability (half dower) but not the divorce itself.

Dispute over Mahr: Evidence, Mutual Oaths, and Mahr al-Mithl
Rulings on mahr dispute when both have evidence, both lack, and role of mahr al-mithl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for resolving mahr disputes when evidence is present and when it is absent, and state the effect of mutual oaths (tahāluf) with the imposition of mahr al-mithl.

Core Idea
Mutual oaths void the specified mahr but not the marriage; mahr al-mithl ensures the wife's right remains intact.

When spouses disagree on the mahr amount, Islamic law provides a structured process. This lesson traces the Hanafi sequence: first evaluate evidence, then apply mutual oaths if evidence is lacking, and finally impose mahr al-mithl. Understanding this process reveals how marriage differs from sale in legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenarios
Determine whether one party has evidence, both have evidence, or both lack evidence. Each scenario triggers a different ruling.
Evidence here means witnesses or written documentation.
2
Evaluate the evidence hierarchy
When both have evidence, the woman's evidence is preferred if the mahr al-mithl supports her claim of increase; the husband's evidence is preferred if the mahr al-mithl supports his claim of decrease. If mahr al-mithl is between the claims, both evidences cancel.
The preference is based on who is claiming against the apparent (zāhir).
3
Apply mutual oaths when evidence is absent or cancelled
If both lack evidence, or if both had evidence but it cancelled, they take mutual oaths (tahāluf). The marriage is not rescinded. If one refuses to swear, the other's claim is accepted.
Mutual oaths negate the specified mahr but do not affect the marriage contract.
4
Impose mahr al-mithl
After mutual oaths, the specified mahr is voided. The wife receives mahr al-mithl, which is the customary dower for a woman of her status. This is imposed because it is known with certainty, unlike the disputed amount.
Mahr al-mithl is determined by factors like family, beauty, education, etc.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that mutual oaths are taken. Abu Yusuf holds that they are not; instead the husband's word with his oath is accepted unless he claims an unreasonably low amount. The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is Abu Hanifa's view.
This difference affects the procedure but not the ultimate validity of the marriage.

Source Text

(عيني) فللمرأة: أي قضي للمرأة؛ لأنها تثبت الزيادة وهذا إذا كان مهر المثل يشهد للزوج بأن كان مثل ما يدعيه أو أقل؛ لأن الظاهر يشهد له وبينة المرأة تثبت خلاف الظاهر فكانت أولى، وإن كان مهر المثل يشهد لها بأن كان مثل ما تدعيه أو أكثر كانت بيئة الزوج أولى؛ لأنها تثبت الحط، وهو خلاف الظاهر والبينات للإثبات، وإن كان مهر مثلها لا يشهد لها ولا له بأن كان أقل مما ادعت المرأة، وأكثر مما ادعاه الزوج، فالصحيح أنهما يتساقطان؛ لأنهما استويا في الإثبات؛ لأن بينتها تثبت الزيادة وبينته تثبت الحط فلا تكون إحداهما أولى من الأخرى. [رمز الحقائق: ١٩٧/٢] (فتح، زيلعي) وإن عجزا: [ أي الزوجان عن إقامة البينة. (عيني)] تحالفا وأيهما نكل لزمه دعوى الآخر؛ لأنه صار مقراً بما يدعيه خصمه أو باذلا. ولم يفسخ النكاح لأن أثر التحالف في عدم التسمية وهو لا يخل لصحة النكاح؛ لأن يمين كل منهما تبطل ما يدعيه صاحبه من التسمية، فيبقى العقد بلا تسمية وهو لا يفسد النكاح؛ إذ المهر تابع فيه فلا حاجة إلى الفسخ بخلاف البيع. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۹۷/۲] لأن الفسخ في البيع بعد التحالف إنما كان لبقاء العقد بلا بدل والنكاح ليس كذلك؛ لأن له موجباً أصلياً يصار إليه عند انعدام التسمية، ثم هذا التحالف عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف: لا يتحالفان والقول قول الزوج مع يمينه إلا أن يأتي بشيء مستنكر لا يتعارف مهراً لها وقد مر تحقيقه في النكاح. (عيني، فتح) بل يحكم مهر المثل: بتشديد الكاف أي يجعل حكما . (عيني)] لقائل أن يقول: ما بالهم لا يحكمون قيمة المبيع إذا اختلف المتبايعان في الثمن لمعرفة من يشهد له الظاهر كما في النكاح فإنه لا محظور فيه، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن مهر المثل أمر معلوم ثابت بيقين فجاز أن يكون حكماً، بخلاف القيمة فإنها تعلم بالتخمين والظن فلا تفيد المعرفة فلا تجعل حكماً، ثم تقديم التحالف على التحكيم هو قول الكرخي ووجهه أن مهر المثل لا اعتبار له مع وجود التسمية؛ لأنه موجب نكاح لا تسمية فيه وسقوط اعتبار التسمية بالتحالف، فلهذا يقدم التحالف فيقضى بقوله لو كان كما قال أو أقل، وبقولها لو كان كما قالت أو أكثر، وبه لو بينهما. الزوج مهر المثل الزوج أي ويقضى بقول المرأة ولو اختلفا في الإجارة قبل الاستيفاء تحالفا، وبعده لا، المؤجر والمستأجر أي يتحالفان المرأة أي ويقضى بمهر المثل - في الوجوه كلها يعني فيما إذا كان مهر المثل مثل ما اعترف به الزوج أو أقل منه أو مثل ما ادعت المرأة أو أكثر منه، أو كان بينهما فهي خمسة وجوه، وعند الرازي: يحكم مهر المثل ثم يتحالفان ولا تحالف إلا في وجه واحد عنده وهو إذا لم يكن مهر المثل شاهداً لأحدهما وفيما عداه فالقول قول الزوج بيمينه إذا كان مهر المثل ما يقوله أو أقل، وقول المرأة يمينها إذا مع كان مثل ما ادعته أو أكثر وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي: الأصح قول الكرخي. (عيني، فتح، عناية) منه، فيقضى بقوله : لأنه لما انتفى بينهما التسمية احتيج إلى تحكيم مهر المثل، فيقضى بقول من يشهد له مهر المثل، وإن لم يشهد لواحد منهما بأن كان أقل مما ادعت وأكثر مما أقر به هو قضي بمهر المثل. (عيني، فتح، عناية) لو بينهما: أي لو كان مهر المثل بين قوليهما بأن كان أكثر مما قال الزوج وأقل مما قالت المرأة. (ط)
And if they dispute over the mahr, judgment is given to the one who produces evidence, and if both produce evidence, then for the woman. And if either of the spouses is unable, they take mutual oaths, and the marriage is not rescinded but rather the mahr al-mithl is adjudged. Explanation: Regarding mahr, i.e., its amount, if the husband says the mahr is one thousand and the woman says two thousand... [continues]
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage covers the detailed rulings on dispute over mahr (dower) in marriage. It states that if one party has evidence, judgment is for that party. If both have evidence, the woman's evidence is preferred because she claims an increase, and if the mahr al-mithl supports the husband, his evidence is preferred if he claims less. If mahr al-mithl supports neither, both evidences cancel out. If both lack evidence, they take mutual oaths (tahāluf). Crucially, unlike in a sale, the marriage is not rescinded; instead, mahr al-mithl is imposed. This is because the effect of the oaths is to nullify the specified mahr, but the marriage remains valid as the mahr is not a pillar. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that mutual oaths are taken; Abu Yusuf holds that they are not, and the husband's word with his oath is accepted unless he claims something unreasonable. The passage then discusses why mahr al-mithl is used instead of market value (like in sale), because mahr al-mithl is known with certainty. The preferred order is to first apply mutual oaths, then resort to mahr al-mithl, according to al-Karkhi. Various scenarios (five faces) are explained.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower that is customary for a woman of similar status to the wife, used when the specified mahr is invalidated.
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by two disputants when each swears to his claim and the other denies, leading to negation of the specified term.
تسمية (tasmiyah): The specification of the mahr amount in the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over mahr where both spouses lack evidence, according to Abu Hanifa, what is the effect of taking mutual oaths?
A) The marriage is rescinded.
B) The specified mahr is voided but the marriage continues with mahr al-mithl.
C) The husband's statement is accepted with his oath.
D) The wife's statement is accepted.

Key Takeaway
When evidence is absent, mutual oaths void the specified mahr but the marriage continues with mahr al-mithl imposed by custom.

Mahr Disputes Without Specification: Five Scenarios and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr dispute without specified dower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr disputes without specified amount, identify the five scenarios based on mahr al-mithl, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between al-Karkhi and al-Razi regarding mutual oath-taking.

Core Idea
When mahr is unspecified, mahr al-mithl decides whose word is taken—no mutual oaths needed unless mahr al-mithl supports neither.

This lesson addresses a common marital dispute: when a husband and wife disagree over the dower amount because no specific mahr was named in the contract. The Hanafi school provides a clear framework using the fair dower (mahr al-mithl) as an objective reference. However, a disagreement arose among later Hanafi scholars on the procedural role of mutual oath-taking (tahalf). This lesson unpacks the mainstream position and its critique by al-Razi, and explains why al-Sarakhsi upheld al-Karkhi's view.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of mahr al-mithl
When no mahr is specified, the judge resorts to mahr al-mithl (fair dower) as an objective standard. The ruling depends on how the parties' claims compare to this standard.
Mahr al-mithl is determined by looking at the dower of women similar to the wife in family, age, beauty, etc.
2
Enumerate the five possible scenarios
The passage lists five possibilities: (a) mahr al-mithl equals husband's acknowledgment or less, (b) mahr al-mithl equals wife's claim or more, (c) mahr al-mithl is between the two statements, and also the cases where it exactly matches one party's claim are included in (a) and (b). So indeed five aspects (wujuh khamsa).
Precisely: (1) mahr al-mithl = husband's said amount, (2) mahr al-mithl  wife's said amount, (5) mahr al-mithl is between the two claims.
3
Apply the mainstream Hanafi ruling (al-Karkhi) to each scenario
In the first two scenarios where mahr al-mithl supports the husband (equals or less), accept his statement with oath. In the next two where it supports the wife (equals or more), accept her statement with oath. In the fifth scenario where it lies between, impose mahr al-mithl directly without oath.
No mutual oath-taking (tahalf) in any scenario according to al-Karkhi.
4
Describe al-Razi's dissenting view
Al-Razi introduces mutual oath-taking before resorting to mahr al-mithl. According to him, tahalf is required in all cases except when mahr al-mithl clearly supports one party. In that exceptional case, the supported party's statement is taken with his/her oath. If mahr al-mithl does not support either, then tahalf occurs and mahr al-mithl is imposed after oaths.
This approach analogizes to other contractual disputes where oaths are used when evidence is lacking.
5
Analyse the Hanafi response and the tarjih
Al-Sarakhsi explicitly states that the correct view is that of al-Karkhi: no need for mutual oaths because mahr al-mithl serves as an objective indicator. The practical difference (thamarah) is that under al-Razi's view, there could be a scenario where tahalf leads to annulment of marriage? The passage does not clarify fully, but the mainstream avoids this complication.
Reasoning (ta'lil): Since no mahr was specified, we must use mahr al-mithl, which then testifies for one party, making oaths unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقضى بقوله لو كان كما قال أو أقل، وبقولها لو كان كما قالت أو أكثر، وبه لو بينهما. الزوج مهر المثل الزوج أي ويقضى بقول المرأة - في الوجوه كلها يعني فيما إذا كان مهر المثل مثل ما اعترف به الزوج أو أقل منه أو مثل ما ادعت المرأة أو أكثر منه، أو كان بينهما فهي خمسة وجوه، وعند الرازي: يحكم مهر المثل ثم يتحالفان ولا تحالف إلا في وجه واحد عنده وهو إذا لم يكن مهر المثل شاهداً لأحدهما وفيما عداه فالقول قول الزوج بيمينه إذا كان مهر المثل ما يقوله أو أقل، وقول المرأة يمينها إذا مع كان مثل ما ادعته أو أكثر وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي: الأصح قول الكرخي. (عيني، فتح، عناية) منه، فيقضى بقوله : لأنه لما انتفى بينهما التسمية احتيج إلى تحكيم مهر المثل، فيقضى بقول من يشهد له مهر المثل، وإن لم يشهد لواحد منهما بأن كان أقل مما ادعت وأكثر مما أقر به قضي بمهر المثل. (عيني، فتح، عناية) لو بينهما: أي لو كان مهر المثل بين قوليهما بأن كان أكثر مما قال الزوج وأقل مما قالت المرأة. (ط)
So judgment is given according to his statement if it is as he said or less, and according to her statement if it is as she said or more, and according to what is between them if it is between them. The husband [gets] the fair dower of the wife—that is, judgment is given by the woman's statement. In all aspects: meaning when the fair dower is like what the husband acknowledged or less, or like what the woman claimed or more, or is between them; these are five aspects. According to al-Razi: The fair dower is judged, then they take oaths, and there is no mutual oath-taking except in one aspect according to him: when the fair dower does not testify for either of them. In other than that, the statement is the husband's with his oath if the fair dower is what he says or less, and the woman's statement with her oath if it is like what she claimed or more. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi said: The correct is the statement of al-Karkhi. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) From him, judgment is given by his statement: because when the specification [of dower] is absent between them, recourse to the fair dower is needed, so judgment is given by the statement of the one whom the fair dower supports. If it does not support either of them—being less than what she claimed and more than what he acknowledged—then judgment is by the fair dower. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'If between them': i.e., if the fair dower is between their statements, being more than what the husband said and less than what the woman said. (T)
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a dispute between spouses regarding the dower when no specific amount was named in the marriage contract, along with an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the procedure. The core ruling: the judge accepts the statement of the party whose claimed amount aligns with the fair dower (mahr al-mithl). Five scenarios are possible: (1) fair dower equals husband's acknowledgment or less → husband's statement accepted; (2) fair dower equals wife's claim or more → wife's statement accepted; (3) fair dower is between them (more than husband said, less than wife claimed) → the fair dower itself is imposed. Al-Razi held a different view: he requires mutual oath-taking (tahalf) in all cases first, then imposes mahr al-mithl only if the fair dower does not support either party. Otherwise, he prefers the statement of the party supported by the fair dower. Imam al-Sarakhsi reports that the correct position is that of al-Karkhi, which is the mainstream Hanafi view: no mutual oath-taking; instead, the fair dower is used as a reference to determine whose statement prevails. The reasoning (ta'lil) given: since no dower was specified, the fair dower is resorted to, and the statement of the one whom it supports is taken. If it supports neither, the fair dower itself becomes the adjudicated amount.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The fair dower equivalent to what women of similar status receive, used when no dower is specified.
تحالف (tahalf): Mutual oath-taking between parties when each claims differently and neither has evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (Karkhi/Sarakhsi), what happens when husband and wife dispute the mahr amount without any evidence, and the fair dower (mahr al-mithl) is exactly what the husband claimed?
A) They take mutual oaths, then the contract is annulled.
B) The judge accepts the husband's statement with his oath.
C) The wife's statement is accepted because she is usually owed mahr.
D) The fair dower is imposed directly.

Key Takeaway
In mahr disputes without specification, the Hanafi school relies on mahr al-mithl as an objective benchmark, and the preferred view does not require mutual oaths.

Dispute Between Spouses Over Household Goods
Ruling on whose statement is accepted for household items based on suitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for disputes between spouses over household goods and determine which spouse's statement is accepted based on customary suitability.

Core Idea
For household goods, each spouse's statement is accepted for items customarily suitable to them, supported by oath and apparent evidence.

When a husband and wife dispute over the ownership of household items and neither has evidence, how does Islamic law resolve the conflict? The Hanafi school provides a clear principle based on customary suitability (shahadat al-zahir). This lesson explains that rule and its applications, including exceptions for the house itself and claims of purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The spouses disagree on the ownership of household items (متاع البيت) and there is no evidence (بينة) from either side. This rule applies whether the marriage is ongoing or dissolved.
The house itself is not included in 'household goods'.
2
Determine suitability of each item
Classify each disputed item based on what is customary for men or women. Items like turbans, swords, books are masculine; items like khimars, bracelets, wraps are feminine.
Custom ('urf) is the benchmark, not personal preference.
3
Apply the rule: statement with oath
The spouse for whom the item is suitable gives their statement with an oath (يمين). That statement is accepted regarding ownership of that item. This is due to apparent evidence (شهادة الظاهر).
If the item suits both or neither, additional rules apply (not detailed here).
4
Note exceptions and refinements
The house itself is presumed for the husband. If one spouse claims acquisition from the other, evidence is required. The rule applies regardless of religion, slave status, or age (if the minor is capable of intercourse).
These exceptions are explicitly mentioned in the text.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The Hanafi method relies on what is outwardly apparent (ظاهر) rather than requiring proof for every item. This facilitates resolution in domestic disputes.
This is a procedural ruling, not a substantive ownership determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختلف الزوجان في متاع البيت فالقول لكل منهما فيما صلح له، = للمستأجر مع . يمينه ؛ لأنه في الشيء الذي أي كل واحد منهما أما المعقود عليه هو المنفعة؛ فلأنه عرض لا يبقى زمانين وأما القيمة فلأن المنافع لا تتقوم إلا بالعقد، وتبين بحلفهما أن لا عقد بينهما لانفساخه من الأصل، فلا يكون لها قيمة يرد عليها الفسخ، وإذا امتنع التحالف فالقول هو المستحق عليه، ولأن جريان التحالف لأجل الفسخ والمنافع التي استوفاها لا يمكن فسخ العقد فيها، فلم يثبت التحالف. (عيني، فتح، عناية) والقول للمستأجر : أي يكون القول للمستأجر مع يمينه (عيني) والبعض معتبر بالكل: [ أي استيفاء بعض المنافع يعتبر بالكل حتى يمنع التحالف في المستوفى ويجري التحالف في الباقي. (عيني)] يعني إذا اختلفا بعد استيفاء بعض المنفعة تحالفا وفسخ العقد فيما بقي، وهذا بالإجماع، فأبو يوسف مر على أصله في هلاك بعض المبيع، فإن التحالف فيه يتقدر بقدر الباقي عنده فكذا هنا، وهما خالفا أصلهما في البيع. والفرق لمحمد: أن المنافع لا تتقوم إلا بالعقد، فلو تحالفا لا يبقى العقد فلم يكن إيجاب شيء، والفرق لأبي حنيفة: أن العقد في الإجارة ينعقد ساعة فساعة على حسب حدوث المنافع، فيصير كل جزء من المنافع كالمعقود عليه عقداً مبتدءاً على حدة، فلا يلزم من تعذر التحالف في الماضي للتعذر فيما بقي؛ إذ هما في حكم عقدين مختلفين، فيتحالفان فيه بخلاف ما إذا ملك بعض المبيع حيث يمنع التحالف؛ لأنه عقد ،واحد فإذا امتنع في البعض امتنع في الكل ضرورة، وأما الماضي فإن المنافع الماضية هالكة، فكان الاختلاف بالنسبة إليها بعد الاستيفاء، ولا تحالف فيه كما مر ، فيكون القول فيه للمستأجر مع يمينه بالاتفاق. (عيني، فتح، عناية) وإن اختلف إلخ: سواء كان الاختلاف حال قيام النكاح أو بعد فسخه وسواء كانا مسلمين أو الزوج مسلم والمرأة ،ذمية، وسواء كانا مملوكين أو المكاتبين أو كبيرين أو صغيرين أو كان الصغير يجامع أي إذا ادعى كل واحد من الزوجين أن المتاع له ولا بينة والمراد من متاع البيت متاع بيت يسكنان فيه، فلو اختلفا في متاع بيت خاص للزوج تسكن المرأة فيه فهو للزوج بالاتفاق، ولو في بيت خاص للمرأة فهو للمرأة اتفاقاً، والتقييد بمتاع البيت للاحتراز عن نفس البيت فإن القول فيه للزوج إلا أن يكون لها بينة. (فتح) لكل منهما إلخ [ أي من الزوجين سواء كان النكاح قائماً بينهما أو لم يكن. (عيني)] محمول على ما إذا ادعى كل واحد منهما الملك المطلق، فلو ادعى أحدهما تلقي الملك من الآخر لشراء أو هبة لا يقبل قوله بمجرد دعواه بل لابد من بينة يقيمها، والذي يصلح للزوج كالعمامة والقباء، والقلنسوة والطيلسان والسلاح والمنطقة، والكتب والقوس، والدرع من الحديد فالقول فيها للزوج مع يمينه بشهادة الظاهر وما يصلح للنساء كالخمار والوقاية، والدرع والأساور، والملحفة والملاءة، ونحو ذلك، فالقول فيها قولها مع يمينها بشهادة الظاهر إلا إذا كان الزوج -
And if the spouses dispute over the household goods, then the statement is for each of them regarding what is suitable for him/her. ... The statement is for the lessee with his oath; because he is regarding the thing which—each one of them— ... The subject matter is the benefit; because it is an accident that does not last for two moments. And the value, because benefits are not valuable except by the contract. And it becomes clear by their oaths that there is no contract between them because it is annulled from the root, so there is no value upon which annulment can return. And when mutual oath is prevented, the statement is of the one against whom the right is claimed. And because the purpose of mutual oath is for annulment, and the benefits that have been utilized cannot have the contract annulled in them, so mutual oath is not established. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) And the statement is for the lessee: i.e., the statement is for the lessee with his oath. (Ayni) And a part is considered like the whole: i.e., utilization of some benefits is considered like the whole such that it prevents mutual oath in the utilized portion, but mutual oath occurs in the remaining portion. (Ayni) Meaning, if they dispute after utilization of some of the benefit, they take mutual oaths and annul the contract regarding what remains; this is by consensus. Abu Yusuf follows his principle regarding destruction of part of the sold item, for mutual oath in that case is measured by the remaining portion according to him, so here too. And they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) contravened their principle in sale. The distinction according to Muhammad: benefits are not valuable except by the contract, so if they take mutual oaths, the contract does not remain, so no obligation of anything. The distinction according to Abu Hanifa: the lease contract is concluded moment by moment according to the occurrence of benefits, so each part of the benefits becomes like a subject matter of a separate contract. So the impossibility of mutual oath in the past does not necessitate impossibility in what remains; they are like two different contracts, so they take mutual oaths regarding it, unlike if part of the sold item is destroyed where mutual oath is prevented because it is one contract; if it is prevented in part, it is prevented in the whole necessarily. As for the past, the past benefits are destroyed, so the dispute regarding them is after utilization, and there is no mutual oath in that as previously mentioned, so the statement regarding it is for the lessee with his oath by agreement. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) And if they differ etc.: This applies whether the dispute occurs during the marriage or after its dissolution, whether both are Muslims or the husband is Muslim and the wife is a dhimmi, whether both are slaves or mukatibs, whether they are adults or minors, or whether the minor has intercourse. That is, when each of the spouses claims that the goods belong to him and there is no evidence. The meaning of 'household goods' is the goods of the house in which they both reside. If they dispute over goods of a house specifically belonging to the husband in which the wife resides, it is for the husband by agreement. If in a house specifically for the wife, it is for the wife by agreement. The restriction to 'household goods' is to exclude the house itself, for the statement regarding the house is for the husband unless she has evidence. (Fath) 'For each of them' etc.: i.e., from the two spouses, whether the marriage is ongoing or not. (Ayni) It is interpreted as when each claims absolute ownership. If one claims that he acquired ownership from the other by purchase or gift, his statement is not accepted by mere claim; rather he must produce evidence. What is suitable for the husband, such as the turban, the jubba, the cap, the cloak, weapons, the belt, books, the bow, and the iron armor—the statement regarding these is for the husband with his oath due to apparent evidence. What is suitable for women, such as the khimar, the face veil, the [؟], bracelets, the sheet, the wrap, and the like—the statement regarding these is for the wife with her oath due to apparent evidence, unless the husband - (cut off)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for disputes between spouses over the ownership of household goods (mut'ah al-bayt) when neither has evidence. The general principle: each spouse's statement is accepted regarding items that are customarily suitable for them. For example, items like a turban, sword, or books are typically masculine, so the husband's statement is taken with his oath; items like a khimar, veil, or jewelry are typically feminine, so the wife's statement is taken. This is based on what is apparent (shahadat al-zahir). The passage also addresses various conditions: the ruling applies whether the marriage is ongoing or dissolved, whether the spouses are Muslims or one is a dhimmi, whether they are free or slaves, and regardless of age. It excludes the house itself—the house is presumed to belong to the husband unless the wife provides evidence. It also clarifies that if one spouse claims to have acquired the item from the other, mere claim is insufficient; evidence is required. The commentary also includes a lengthy discussion (from the earlier ijarah section) on partial utilization and its effect on mutual oath, which serves as a reminder that the same principle applies to the ijarah context but also relates to the general theme of tahalf. The passage ends abruptly, but the ruling on household goods is clear.

Key Terms
متاع البيت (mata' al-bayt): Household goods and furniture, as opposed to the house itself.
شهادة الظاهر (shahadat al-zahir): What is apparent from custom or common usage; used as a basis for accepting one spouse's statement over the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If husband and wife dispute ownership of a silver bracelet without evidence, according to Hanafi fiqh, whose statement is accepted?
A) The husband's, because he is the owner of the house.
B) The wife's, because a bracelet is typically for women.
C) They must divide the bracelet equally.
D) The judge decides based on which spouse is more trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over household goods with no evidence, each spouse's statement with oath is accepted for items customarily suitable to them.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Resolving Spousal Disputes Over Household Goods
Procedural ruling for qadis on spousal property disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principles of yad (possession) and shahadat al-zahir (apparent evidence) to resolve disputes between spouses over ownership of household items, including gender-specific and shared items, and explain the ruling after one spouse's death.

Core Idea
For shared goods, the husband's hand prevails; gender-specific items go to the customarily suitable spouse.

When spouses disagree over who owns the household items, the Hanafi school provides a clear framework based on possession (yad) and customary usage (shahadat al-zahir). This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, expanded by later commentators, lays out the default rules in the absence of evidence. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone studying Islamic family law or aspiring to serve as a qadi or mufti.

Guided Path
1
Categorise the disputed item
Classify the item as suitable only for the husband, only for the wife, or suitable for both. This is the first step because the ruling depends on the category.
Examples: turban (husband), khimar (wife), blanket (both).
2
Apply shahadat al-zahir for gender-specific items
For items suitable only for one gender, the decisive word is given to that spouse with an oath, because apparent usage (such as wearing) is a strong indicator of ownership.
The spouse does not need to provide a witness; the custom itself is the shahada.
3
Apply the rule of yad for items suitable for both
For items that could belong to either spouse, the husband gets the decisive word with an oath. This is because the wife's possessions are considered under the husband's hand (yad) as head of the household, and the party in possession prevails.
If both produce evidence, the wife's evidence is preferred because it is external (she brought it from her family).
4
Consider special circumstances: post-death and slave status
If one spouse dies, the surviving spouse takes all shared items (Abu Hanifa's view), but note the seven other opinions. If one spouse is a slave, the free spouse gets the items because the slave's hand is not a hand of full ownership.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree on the slave's hand, equating a mukatab or trade-permitted slave to a free person.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad would give more weight to custom (e.g., the wife's trousseau) in some cases, potentially overriding the husband's yad. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position, but the difference shows the school's internal range.
The seven opinions after death (muthanna) are also a classic example of ikhtilaf within and outside the school.

(فتح) لكل منهما إلخ [ أي من الزوجين سواء كان النكاح قائماً بينهما أو لم يكن. (عيني)] محمول على ما إذا ادعى كل واحد منهما الملك المطلق، فلو ادعى أحدهما تلقي الملك من الآخر لشراء أو هبة لا يقبل قوله بمجرد دعواه بل لابد من بينة يقيمها، والذي يصلح للزوج كالعمامة والقباء، والقلنسوة والطيلسان والسلاح والمنطقة، والكتب والقوس، والدرع من الحديد فالقول فيها للزوج مع يمينه بشهادة الظاهر وما يصلح للنساء كالخمار والوقاية، والدرع والأساور، والملحفة والملاءة، ونحو ذلك، فالقول فيها قولها مع يمينها بشهادة الظاهر إلا إذا كان الزوج - وله فيما صلح لهما، فإن مات أحدهما، فللحي، ولو أحدهما مملوكا، أي في الشيء أحكام التحالف لو كان أحد الزوجين واختلفا في متاع البيت هذه الأشياء، فلا يكون القول لها لتعارض الظاهر، وكذا إذا كانت المرأة تبيع ما يصلح للرجال لا يكون يبيع القول قوله في ذلك. وهذا إذا لم تقل المرأة أن هذا المتاع اشتراه الزوج فإن أقرت بذلك سقط قولها؛ لأنها أقرت بالملك لزوجها ثم ادعت الانتقال إليها فلا يثبت الانتقال إلا بالبينة [ رمز الحقائق: ۱۹۸/۲] (فتح) وله أي وإن اختلفا في شيء يصلح للزوجين كالفرش والأواني والأمتعة والرقيق والمنزل والعقار والمواشي والنقود فالقول للزوج مع يمينه؛ لأن المرأة وما في يدها في يد الزوج، وإذا تنازع اثنان في شيء وهو في يد أحدهما كان القول لصاحب اليد فكذا هنا، بخلاف ما يختص بالمرأة؛ لأن لها ظاهراً آخر أظهر من اليد وهو يد الاستعمال فجعل القول لها، ولو أقاما بينة فيما صلح لهما يقضى ببينتها؛ لأنها خارجة، ولو أبدل المصنف هذه العبارة بقوله: وإن اختلف الزوجان في متاع البيت فلها ما صلح لها وله ما صلح له أولهما لكان أخصر. (عيني، فتح) فإن مات إلخ أي لو مات أحد الزوجين واختلف ورثة الميت مع الحي في المتاع فيما يصلح لهما فهو للحي منهما أيهما كان في قول أبي حنيفة؛ إذ لا يد للميت فبقيت يد الحي بلا معارض وفي هذه المسألة سبعة أقوال؛ ولهذا تسمى مسبعة كتاب الدعوى وقد ذكر الأول منها. وقال أبو يوسف يدفع للمرأة ما يجهز به مثلها؛ لأنها تأتي بالجهاز عادة فكان الظاهر شاهداً لها وهو أقوى من ظاهر يد الزوج فيبطل به ظاهره، ولا معارض في الباقي فيعتبر، فيكون الباقي للزوج مع يمينه للظاهر ولورثته بعد موته. وقال محمد: يصرف إليه في حياته، وإلى ورثته بعد موته؛ لأن يد ورثته كيده، وقال زفر: ما يصلح لهما يكون مقسوماً بينهما نصفان بمنزلة عين في يد اثنين ادعياها وهو رواية عن أحمد ومحمد، وروى الحسن عن زفر أيضاً: أن جميع ما في البيت بينهما إلا ما كان في يده مشاهدة، وبه قال الشافعي ومالك، وقال الحسن البصري: المتاع كله للمرأة، وليس للرجل إلا ما عليه من ثياب بدنه، وقال ابن أبي ليلى المتاع كله للزوج كيف ما كان، فهذه سبعة أقاويل، وأما ما يصلح لأحدهما ولا يصلح للآخر فهو بناء على ما كان قبل الموت وتقوم ورثته مقامه فيه بالاتفاق. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۹۸/۲] (فتح) ولو أحدهما إلخ أي لو اختلف الزوجان في متاع البيت وأحدهما مملوك، سواء كان محجوراً أو مأذوناً له أو مكاتباً، فالمتاع يكون للحر إذا كانا حيين؛ لأن يد الحر أقوى؛ لأنها يد ملك، ولا كذلك يد المملوك، وهذا على إطلاقه قول أبي حنيفة، وقالا : العبد المأذون له في التجارة والمكاتب كالحر؛ لأن لهما يداً معتبرة في الخصومات، ولهذا لو اختصم الحر والمكاتب في شيء في أيديهما، يقضى به بينهما؛ لاستوائهما في اليد، ولو كان في يد ثالث وأقاما البينة استويا فيه، فكما لا يترجح الحر بالحرية في سائر الخصومات فكذلك في متاع البيت، والجواب أن اليد على متاع البيت باعتبار السكنى فيه والحر أصل دون المملوك فلا تعارض بينهما، وبقولهما قال الشافعي ومالك. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And if the spouses disagree about the household goods, then each has the say in what is suitable for him/her. 'And if they disagree etc.': Whether the disagreement occurs during the marriage or after its dissolution, whether both are Muslims or the husband is Muslim and the wife is a dhimmi, whether both are slaves or kitabis or adults or minors or if the minor has intercourse, i.e., when each of the spouses claims that the goods belong to him/her and there is no evidence. The meaning of household goods is the goods of the house they both inhabit. So if they disagree about the goods of a house specifically for the husband where the wife resides, it belongs to the husband by agreement, and if in a house specifically for the wife, it belongs to the wife by agreement. The restriction to household goods is to exclude the house itself, for the decisive word in that is for the husband unless she has evidence. (Fath) 'Each of them etc.' [i.e., from the two spouses whether the marriage is present or not. (Ayni)] It applies when each claims absolute ownership. So if one claims to have acquired ownership from the other by purchase or gift, his statement is not accepted by mere claim; rather, he must provide evidence. Items suitable for the husband, such as the turban, jubba, cap, taylasan, weapon, belt, books, bow, iron armor, the decisive word is for the husband with his oath due to apparent evidence (shahadat al-zahir). Items suitable for women, such as the khimar, protective veil, armor, bracelets, blanket, sheet, and the like, the decisive word is for her with her oath due to apparent evidence, unless the husband - and for him regarding what is suitable for both. If one of them dies, it is for the living. And if one of them is a slave, i.e., regarding the things - rulings of tahaluf if one of the spouses is a slave and they disagree about household goods these things, then the decisive word is not for her due to conflicting apparent evidence. Similarly, if the woman sells what is suitable for men, her statement is not accepted in that. And this is if the woman does not say that the husband bought these goods; if she admits that, her statement becomes void because she admitted the husband's ownership and then claimed transfer to herself; transfer is not established except by evidence. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 198/2] (Fath) And for him, i.e., if they disagree about something suitable for both, such as bedding, vessels, furniture, slaves, house, real estate, livestock, cash, then the decisive word is for the husband with his oath because the woman and what is in her hand is in the hand of the husband. When two contest over something and it is in the hand of one, the decisive word is for the possessor; so here. Unlike what is specific to women, because she has another apparent indication stronger than the hand, which is the hand of use, so the decisive word is given to her. If they both produce evidence for what is suitable for both, it is decided by the wife's evidence because it is external. If the author had replaced this expression with his saying: 'And if the spouses disagree about household goods, then for her what is suitable for her and for him what is suitable for him or for both' it would be more concise. (Ayni, Fath) 'If one dies etc.': i.e., if one of the spouses dies and the heirs of the deceased disagree with the survivor about the goods regarding what is suitable for both, then it is for the survivor, whichever it is, according to Abu Hanifa's opinion, because the deceased has no hand, so the hand of the survivor remains without opposition. In this issue there are seven opinions; therefore it is called the 'septenary' of the Book of Claims. And he mentioned the first of them. Abu Yusuf said: The woman is given what is typically equipped for her like, because she usually brings the trousseau, so the apparent evidence is a witness for her, which is stronger than the apparent hand of the husband, so it nullifies his apparent evidence. For the remainder, there is no opposition, so it is considered, so the remainder goes to the husband with his oath for the apparent evidence, and to his heirs after his death. Muhammad said: It is given to him during his life, and to his heirs after his death, because the hand of his heirs is like his hand. Zufar said: What is suitable for both is divided between them half and half, like an item in the hands of two each claiming it; this is also narrated from Ahmad and Muhammad. And al-Hasan narrated from Zufar also: All that is in the house is shared between them except what is in his hand observably; this is what al-Shafi'i and Malik said. Al-Hasan al-Basri said: The goods are all for the woman, and the man has nothing except the clothes on his body. Ibn Abi Layla said: The goods are all for the husband, however that may be. These are seven opinions. As for what is suitable for one and not the other, it is based on what was before death, and his heirs stand in his place in it by agreement. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 198/2] (Fath) 'And if one of them etc.': i.e., if the spouses disagree about household goods and one of them is a slave, whether the slave is restricted or permitted to trade or a mukatab, then the goods belong to the free person if both are alive, because the hand of the free person is stronger, being a hand of ownership, and not like the hand of the slave. This is, in its absolute sense, the opinion of Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The slave permitted to trade and the mukatab are like the free person because they have a hand that is considered in lawsuits; therefore, if a free person and a mukatab dispute over something in their hands, it is decided between them due to equality in possession. If it is in a third hand and they produce evidence, they are equal in it. So just as the free person is not given preference over the slave in other lawsuits, likewise in household goods. The answer is that possession over household goods is due to residence in it, and the free person is the original, not the slave, so there is no conflict between them. Their opinion is also the opinion of al-Shafi'i and Malik. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on disputes between spouses over household goods. It first states the general rule: in the absence of evidence, each spouse gets what is customarily suitable for them (e.g., husband gets men's items, wife gets women's items). For items suitable for both, the husband's statement is taken with his oath because he has the hand of possession (yad). The commentary then elaborates: the dispute covers all situations (during marriage or after, different religions, ages, etc.). If one claims transfer from the other, evidence is required. The 'shahadat al-zahir' (apparent evidence) based on usage is applied. For items suitable for both, the husband's hand is the deciding factor. After one spouse dies, the surviving spouse gets all shared items according to Abu Hanifa (since the deceased has no hand), but there are seven other opinions from other imams (the so-called 'muthanna' of the Book of Claims). Finally, if one spouse is a slave, Abu Hanifa rules that the free spouse gets the goods because the slave's hand is not a hand of ownership, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the slave's hand valid if the slave is permitted to trade or is a mukatab.

Key Terms
متاع البيت (mata' al-bayt): Household goods or furniture and items found in a house that a couple shares.
شهادة الظاهر (shahadat al-zahir): Apparent evidence or inference based on external circumstances, such as gender-typical clothing suggesting ownership.
اليد (al-yad): Physical possession or control, considered prima facie evidence of ownership in disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a household item suitable for both men and women (e.g., a carpet), who has the decisive word in the absence of evidence?
A) The wife, because she is usually in charge of such items.
B) The husband, because he has the hand of possession as the head of household.
C) Both equally, so it is divided.
D) The qadi assigns it based on the higher value.

Key Takeaway
When spouses dispute household goods without evidence, Hanafi law uses apparent usage for gender-specific items and the husband's possession for shared items, reflecting the principle that possession is a strong indicator of ownership.

Nikah — Marriage

Seller's Claim to Child of Sold Slave-Girl: Istihsan over Qiyas
Ruling and conditions for seller's paternity claim, with istihsan vs qiyas dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a seller's paternity claim is valid and explain why the Hanafi school adopts istihsan over qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Certainty of conception in ownership overrides the contradiction of selling the slave-girl.

When a man sells a slave-girl and she gives birth within six months, the seller may claim paternity. This seems contradictory—why sell your own child's mother? The Hanafi school, through istihsan, nevertheless accepts the claim, prioritizing the strong presumption that the child is legitimate. This lesson unpacks the conditions and the legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the first part: a sold slave-girl gives birth within six months; the seller claims the child. What is the immediate ruling? The child is his son and she is umm walad.
2
List the conditions for the claim to be valid
The passage lists: child born within six months, child alive, child not freed or mudabbar by buyer, buyer did not already claim the child. Note also the discussion about the mother's status.
These conditions guard against irrevocable acts.
3
Understand the qiyas objection
Zufar and the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Hasan) argue by qiyas: selling the slave-girl contradicts the claim, so the claim is invalid.
The sale is evidence against paternity.
4
Appreciate the istihsan argument
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan: certainty of conception in his ownership and the default of no adultery create a strong presumption that acts like evidence (bayyinah), overriding the contradiction.
This is a classic Hanafi use of istihsan over qiyas.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the istihsan ruling, the sale is rescinded, the price is returned, and the slave-girl becomes umm walad. Under qiyas, the child stays with the buyer and no paternity is established.
This difference affects both family law and contract law.

(فتح، عناية) فهو ابنه بشرط أن لا يسبقه المشتري في الدعوة والحاصل أن البائع إذا ادعى ولد المبيعة، فلا يخلو إما أن تجيء به لأقل . من سنتين أم لا، ثم ذلك لا يخلو إما أن يصدقه المشتري في الدعوة أم لا، وكل ذلك لا يخلو إما أن يكون الولد المدعى نسبه حياً أو ميتاً، والأول لا يخلو إما أن يوقع المشتري به ما لا يمكن نقضه كالعتق والتدبير، أو ما يمكن كالبيع والكتابة والرهن والإجارة والهبة أم لا. وكذلك الأم على هذا التقسيم إما أن تكون وقت الدعوة حية أو ميتة، فإن كانت حية، فإما أن يكون المشتري أوقع بها ما لم يمكن نقضه أو ما يمكن، فإذا ادعى البائع ولد المبيعة، ينظر إذا جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر، وهو حي، لم يتصف بالعتق أو التدبير، ولم يسبقه المشتري في الدعوة، ثبت النسب من البائع مطلقاً، صدقه المشتري أم لا، فالتقييد بالحياة للاحتراز عن الوفاة، حيث لا يثبت نسبه؛ لأن الحقوق لا يثبت للميت ابتداء ولا عليه، والتقييد بعدم اتصافه بالعتق أي التدبير؛ للاحتراز عما إذا كان الولد عند الدعوة عتيقاً أو مدبراً أن أعتقه المشتري أو دبره حيث لا يثبت نسبه أيضاً؛ لأن ثبوت نسبه يستلزم نقض عتقه أو تدبيره وكل منهما بعد وقوعه لا ينتقض، بخلاف ما إذا ادعى نسبه بعد أن باعه المشتري أو كاتبه أو ،رهنه حيث يثبت نسبه وتنتقض هذه التصرفات، والتقييد بعدم سبق المشتري البائع في الدعوة؛ للاحتراز عما إذا ادعاه قبله فإن النسب يثبت من المشتري، ولا يتصور بعده ثبوت النسب من البائع. وهي أم ولده: أي إذا ثبت نسب الولد من البائع فالولد ابنه، والأمة المبيعة أم ولد البائع استحساناً، والقياس: أن تكون هذه الدعوى باطلة؛ لأن إقدامه على البيع دليل على أن الحمل ليس منه والولد عبد له، فكان في دعواه متناقضاً، فلا تسمع دعواه، فإذا لم تصح الدعوى لم يثبت النسب؛ إذ لا نسب في الجارية بدون الدعوى، وهو قول زفر والثلاثة، وجه الاستحسان أنا تيقنا باتصال العلوق في ملكه، وذلك شهادة ظاهرة على كونه منه؛ لأن الظاهر عدم الزنا، فنزل ذلك منزلة البينة في إبطال حق الغير عنها وعن ولدها، فتصح دعواه مستنداً إلى وقت العلوق، فيفسخ البيع؛ لأنه قد باع أم ولده ويرد الثمن أي ثمن المبيعة إلى المشتري؛ لأنه مقتضى الفسخ في العقد. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
Chapter of Paternity Claim (Da'wa al-Nasab) i.e., explaining its rulings. She gave birth to a sold slave-girl (mabī'a) in less than the period of pregnancy from the time she was sold, and the seller claimed him, so he is his son, and she is his umm walad. i.e., a sold female slave in less than six months, i.e., from the time of sale. The child, the seller, the slave-girl, the seller. Chapter of Paternity Claim: When he finished with claims regarding properties, he began explaining paternity claims, because the former is more frequent, so it was more important to mention, so he preceded it over the latter. And 'da'wa' (invitation) to food is with fatha, and in lineage is with kasra. Then paternity claim is of three types: (1) Claim of istīlād (offspring claim) – which is based on the time of conception in his ownership; (2) Claim of tahrīr (liberation claim) – which is limited and not based on conception in his ownership; (3) Claim of shubha (equivocal claim) – which is the father's claim to the child of his son's slave-girl; lineage is established from him even if his son does not affirm him, on condition that the slave-girl was in the ownership of his son from the time of conception until the time of claim. (Fath, 'Inayah). So he is his son, on condition that the buyer does not precede him in the claim. The summary: when the seller claims the child of the sold slave-girl, it is not free from: either she gives birth in less than two years or not; then that is not free from: either the buyer affirms him in the claim or not; and all that is not free from: either the claimed child is alive or dead; and the first is not free from: either the buyer has done to him something that cannot be undone, like freeing (itq) or tadbīr, or something that can be undone like sale, kitaba, pledge, lease, gift, or not. Similarly, the mother is either alive or dead at the time of claim. If she is alive, then either the buyer has done to her something that cannot be undone or can. So when the seller claims the child of the sold slave-girl, it is considered: if she gives birth in less than six months, and he is alive, not characterized by freeing or tadbīr, and the buyer did not precede him in the claim, then lineage is established from the seller absolutely, whether the buyer affirms him or not. The restriction to life is to guard against death, where his lineage is not established because rights are not established for or against a dead person initially. The restriction to not being characterized by freeing (i.e., tadbīr) is to guard against the case where the child at the time of claim is already free or mudabbar, because establishing his lineage would necessitate revoking his freeing or tadbīr, and each of these, after occurrence, is not revocable. Unlike if he claimed his lineage after the buyer sold him, or made a kitaba contract, or pledged him, where his lineage is established and these dispositions are revoked. And the restriction that the buyer does not precede the seller in the claim is to guard against the case where he claimed him before; then lineage is established from the buyer, and after that, establishment of lineage from the seller is inconceivable. And she is his umm walad: meaning if the lineage of the child is established from the seller, then the child is his son, and the sold slave-girl is the seller's umm walad by istihsan (juristic preference). By qiyas (analogy), this claim would be invalid, because his proceeding with the sale is evidence that the pregnancy is not from him and the child is his slave, so in his claim he is contradictory, so his claim is not heard. If the claim is not valid, lineage is not established, since there is no lineage for the slave-girl without claim. This is the statement of Zufar and the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Hasan). The basis of istihsan: We are certain of the connection of conception in his ownership, and that is a manifest testimony that he is from him, because the default is no adultery. So this takes the place of evidence in nullifying the right of others over her and over her child. Therefore, his claim based on the time of conception is valid, and the sale is rescinded because he has sold his umm walad, and the price (i.e., the price of the sold slave-girl) is returned to the buyer, because that is the consequence of rescinding the contract. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on paternity claims (da'wa al-nasab) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with a specific case: a man sells a slave-girl who later gives birth to a child within six months of the sale (the minimum period of pregnancy being six months). The seller claims the child as his own. The ruling is that if the child is born within six months of the sale, is alive, has not been freed or made mudabbar by the buyer, and the buyer has not already claimed the child, then the child is established as the seller's son and the slave-girl becomes his umm walad (mother of his child, which entails her freedom upon his death). The commentary then discusses the three types of paternity claims: istīlād (based on conception in one's ownership), tahrīr (a claim without basis), and shubha (equivocal claim, e.g., father claiming child of his son's slave-girl). The main part details the conditions for the seller's claim to be valid: the child must be born within six months, be alive, not be freed or mudabbar, and the buyer must not have preceded in the claim. If the child is dead or freed, the claim fails because it would require revoking an irrevocable act. The passage also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: by qiyas (analogy), the claim would be invalid because the seller's act of selling contradicts the claim (Zufar and others). However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the claim is accepted because we are certain that conception occurred while the slave-girl was in the seller's ownership, and the presumption is that the child is legitimate (no adultery). This istihsan overrides the apparent contradiction, and the sale is rescinded as the slave-girl becomes umm walad.

Key Terms
دعوى النسب (da'wa al-nasab): A legal claim to establish lineage/paternity of a child.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her owner; she becomes free upon her owner's death.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; deviating from a clear analogy (qiyas) in favor of a stronger evidence or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the seller's paternity claim to be valid?
A) The child is born within six months of the sale
B) The child is alive at the time of the claim
C) The child has been freed by the buyer
D) The buyer has not already claimed the child

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a seller's paternity claim for a child born within six months by istihsan, overruling the apparent contradiction of the sale, due to the certainty of conception in his ownership and the presumption of legitimacy.

Precedence of Seller's Claim of Lineage
Ruling on seller's claim of lineage and its effect on sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's claim of lineage when a sold slave-girl gives birth, distinguish between istilad and tahrir claims, and apply the rule to scenarios of death or timing.

Core Idea
The seller's claim of lineage (istilad) takes precedence over the buyer's claim (tahrir) because it traces back to conception.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding claims of lineage when a slave-girl is sold and later gives birth. The seller's claim that the child is his from his ownership (istilad) is given precedence over the buyer's claim (tahrir) due to the stronger basis. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the principles of lineage and umm walad, as well as the effect of death of the mother or child on the claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the seller's claim that a child born to a sold slave-girl is his. The ruling is that the sale is rescinded and the price returned if the claim is accepted.
The claim must be based on istilad (conception during his ownership).
2
Distinguish between istilad and tahrir claims
The seller's claim is istilad (going back to conception), while the buyer's claim is tahrir (based on current ownership only). Istilad is stronger because it establishes lineage directly.
The buyer's claim is treated like that of a stranger.
3
Apply the precedence rule
When both seller and buyer claim, the seller's claim takes precedence. The buyer's claim is invalid if made after the seller's claim, and even if made first, the seller's claim may still be considered if the buyer's claim is not established.
The text discusses scenarios where the buyer claims first or after the seller.
4
Handle death scenarios
If the mother dies and the child is born within six months (indicating conception before sale), the seller's claim is accepted. If the child dies, the seller's claim is not accepted because the child no longer needs lineage and the mother cannot become umm walad.
The child is the origin; the mother follows.
5
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Her child frees her' (Ibn Majah) shows that the child is the cause of freedom for the mother. This supports the principle that the child's existence is necessary for istilad.
The hadith is ahad and zanni al-thubut, but used as support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفسخ البيع، ويرد الثمن، وإن ادعاه المشتري معه، أو بعده وكذا إن ماتت الأم، البائع - الولد أي مع دعوى البائع بخلاف موت الولد وعتقهما كموتهما، وإن ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر، المشتري الأم والولد أي الأمة المبيعة وإن ادعاه إلخ واصل بما قبله أي ولو ادعى المشتري الولد مع دعوى البائع أو بعد دعواه تكون دعوى البائع مقدماً؛ لأن دعوى البائع أسبق؛ إذ هي تستند إلى حالة العلوق؛ لكونها دعوة الاستيلاد ودعوة المشتري دعوة التحرير ؛ إذ العلوق لم يكن في ملكه فتقتصر على الحال فكانت دعوة البائع أقوى، فلا تعتبر الثانية، فصار المشتري كغيره من الأجانب، فلا تصح دعواه، بخلاف ما إذا ادعى المشتري أولاً ثم ادعى البائع لا يثبت النسب من البائع؛ لاستغنائه بثبوت نسبه من المشتري، ولأن النسب لا يحتمل الإبطال بل يثبت النسب من المشتري؛ لوجود ملكه، وتثبت أميتها بإقراره، وقيل: يحتمل على أنه نكحها واستولدها ثم اشتراها. (عيني، وفتح) أو بعده: أي إذا ادعى المشتري بعد دعوى البائع، وتذكير الضمير باعتبار الادعاء. (عيني) إن ماتت الأم: أي وكذا يثبت نسب الولد من البائع إن ماتت الأم وادعاه البائع، وقد جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر فيأخذ البائع الولد. (عيني) بخلاف موت الولد أي بخلاف ما إذا ادعاه البائع بعد موت الولد فإنه لا يثبت نسبه منه؛ لأن الولد بعد الموت استغنى عن النسب، ولم تصر الأمة أم ولده؛ لأن الاستيلاد فرع النسب، فإذا لم يثبت النسب لم يثبت الاستيلاد والفرق أن الولد هو الأصل في هذا الباب والأم تبع له ألا ترى أنها تضاف إليه، يقال: أم الولد، ولأنها تستفيد الحرية من جهته. ألا ترى إلى قوله عليها حين ولدت مارية القبطية ولده إبراهيم فقيل له: ألا تعتقها؟ فقال: "أعتقها ولدها"، رواه ابن ماجه، فإذا كان الولد هو الأصل، كان المعتبر بقاؤه؛ لحاجته إلى ثبوت النسب، ولا يضره فوت التبع بخلاف العكس، وهو ما إذا مات الولد دون الأم، حيث لا تصح دعوته في الأم؛ لأن الحكم لا يثبت في التبع ابتداء دون متبوعه.
The sale is rescinded and the price is returned, if the buyer claims [the child] with him or after him, and likewise if the mother dies, the seller [claims] the child, i.e., along with the seller's claim, unlike the death of the child and the freeing of both of them like their death, and if she gives birth after more than six months, the buyer [claims] the mother and the child, i.e., the sold slave-girl. 'And if he claims it etc.' is connected to what preceded, meaning if the buyer claims the child along with the seller's claim or after his claim, the seller's claim is given precedence because the seller's claim is earlier; it goes back to the state of conception being a claim of istilad, while the buyer's claim is a claim of tahrir since the conception did not occur in his ownership, so it is limited to the present state, thus the seller's claim is stronger, so the second is not considered, and the buyer becomes like any other outsider, so his claim is not valid. Unlike if the buyer claimed first then the seller claims, the lineage from the seller is not established because it is sufficient that his lineage is established from the buyer, and lineage does not admit of nullification; rather lineage is established from the buyer due to his ownership, and her being an umm walad is established by his acknowledgement. It is said: it is possible that he married her and had a child with her then bought her. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or after him': i.e., if the buyer claims after the seller's claim, and the masculine pronoun is by consideration of the claim. (Ayni) 'If the mother dies': i.e., likewise the lineage of the child from the seller is established if the mother dies and the seller claims him, and she has given birth to him in less than six months, so the seller takes the child. (Ayni) 'Unlike the death of the child': i.e., unlike if the seller claims him after the death of the child, then his lineage is not established from him because the child after death is independent of lineage, and the slave-girl does not become an umm walad because istilad is a branch of lineage; if lineage is not established, istilad is not established. The difference is that the child is the origin in this section and the mother is a follower of him; do you not see that she is attributed to him, it is said: 'mother of the child', and because she acquires freedom through him. Do you not see his statement, peace be upon him, when Mariya al-Qibtiyya gave birth to his son Ibrahim, and it was said to him: 'Will you not free her?' He said: 'Her child frees her,' reported by Ibn Majah. Since the child is the origin, his existence is considered due to his need for establishment of lineage, and the loss of the follower does not harm him, unlike the opposite, i.e., when the child dies without the mother, then his claim regarding the mother is not valid because the ruling is not established in the follower initially without the follower.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of Claims regarding lineage, specifically when a seller claims that a child born to a sold slave-girl is his own child. The text states that if the seller claims the child, the sale is rescinded and the price returned. If the buyer also claims the child, the seller's claim takes precedence because it goes back to the time of conception (istilad), whereas the buyer's claim is only about liberation (tahrir) from current ownership. Therefore, the buyer is like any other outsider; his claim is invalid. If the mother dies and the child is born within six months (indicating conception before sale), the seller's claim stands. However, if the child dies, the seller's claim is invalid because the child no longer needs lineage, and the mother cannot become umm walad since istilad depends on the child's existence. The text uses a hadith: 'Her child frees her' (Ibn Majah) to show the child is the origin. This is all based on Hanafi principles of lineage and istilad.

Key Terms
دعوى الاستيلاد (da'wa al-istilad): Claim that a child was conceived from one's own ownership/semen, establishing the mother as umm walad.
دعوى التحرير (da'wa al-tahrir): Claim that the child was born free due to current ownership, without establishing lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The claim of the child itself.

Key Takeaway
The seller's claim of lineage (istilad) takes precedence over the buyer's claim (tahrir) because it traces back to conception, and the child's existence is essential for establishing umm walad.
