Mukatab Marrying His Slave Girl: Earning vs Harm
Ruling with talil and intra-school refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab marrying his slave girl and explain why a mukatabah may not marry herself.

Core Idea
Marriage by a mukatab of his slave girl is earning; by a mukatabah it is harm.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq distinguishes two cases: a mukatab marrying his female slave, which is permissible as a means of earning, and a mukatabah marrying herself, which is prohibited because it harms the master. The commentary adds a restriction by al-Qahistani, limiting the permission to marriage to another's slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Note the mukatab (male slave under kitabah) and the mukatabah (female slave under kitabah).
2
State the ruling for each
Mukatab may marry his amah (mubah); mukatabah may not marry herself (haram).
The contrast is explicit in the text.
3
Understand the rationale (talil)
For the mukatab, it is a means of earning (kash). For the mukatabah, it causes a defect to her and harms the master, and her purpose is chastity, not earning.
The talil explains the difference in ruling.
4
Recognize the later restriction
Al-Qahistani restricts the permission to when the mukatab marries her to another's slave, not his own slave, because marrying to his own slave does not bring new wealth.
This is a refinement within the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the principle
The master's interest overrides in the case of the mukatabah; the mukatab's limited ownership allows earning transactions.
Consider the broader principle of 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتزويج يعني للمكاتب أن يزوج أمته؛ لأنه من الاكتساب، فيملكه ضرورة، بخلاف تزويج المكاتبة نفسها حيث لا يجوز لها وإن كان فيه اكتساب؛ لأن ملك المولى باقي فيها، وفي التزويج تعيبها ، وربما يعجز فيبقى هذا العيب، فيكون على المولى ضرر، وليس مقصودها من تزويج نفسها المال، وإنما هو التحصين والإعفاف بخلاف تزويج المكاتب أمته، أمته: فإن المقصود منه كسب المال. [ تكملة البحر الرائق: ۹۱/۸] قال في "الفتح": وهو بإطلاقه شامل لما لو زوجها من عبد نفسه وليس كذلك، ولهذا قيد المسألة القهستاني بما إذا زوجها بعبد غيره.
And marrying off his slave girl: meaning the mukatab may marry off his slave girl, because it is a means of earning, so he owns it by necessity, unlike a mukatabah marrying herself, which is not permissible for her even though it involves earning, because the master's ownership remains in her, and in marriage there is a defect to her, and perhaps she becomes unable and this defect remains, causing harm to the master. Her purpose in marrying herself is not money, but rather chastity and protection, unlike the mukatab marrying off his slave girl. His slave girl: the purpose is earning money. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:91] Al-Fath said: This includes even if he marries her to his own slave, but this is not the case, hence al-Qahistani restricted the issue to when he marries her to another's slave.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the mukatab may marry off his female slave because it is a means of acquiring wealth (kash). In contrast, a mukatabah (female slave under kitabah) may not marry herself because doing so would harm the master: marriage reduces her value, and if she later becomes unable to pay, that defect becomes permanent. Her goal is chastity, not money. The commentary adds that the permission is general, but al-Qahistani restricts it to marriage to another's slave, because marrying her to the mukatab's own slave would not bring new money and may lead to complications.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwīj): The act of giving someone in marriage, here referring to the mukatab marrying off his slave girl.
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by the mukatab.
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A female slave under a kitabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is not allowed to earn money.
B) Because it causes a defect to her and harms the master's interest.
C) Because marriage is not a valid contract for slaves.
D) Because she must first obtain permission from the master, which is unlikely.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab may marry his slave girl for earning; a mukatabah may not marry herself due to harm to the master.

Mukatab's Marriage and Concubinage
Ruling on mukatab's marriage and concubinage with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a mukatab's marriage and tasarrī, and explain the underlying rationale (talil) for each prohibition.

Core Idea
A mukatab may not marry without permission and can never take a concubine due to lacking full ownership.

This lesson examines two important acts for a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission): marriage and taking a concubine. While marriage is permissible with the master's consent, tasarrī is absolutely prohibited even with permission. Understanding the legal reasoning (talil) behind these rulings clarifies the mukatab's limited capacity in property and person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, notice that the passage addresses two distinct acts: marriage (nikah) and taking a concubine (tasarrī). For marriage, the mukatab may not do so without the master's permission; with permission it is valid. For tasarrī, it is never permissible, even with permission.
The text explicitly states 'لا يملك التزوج بلا إذن' and 'وأما التسري فلا يجوز ولو بإذن المولى'.
2
Understand the rationale for marriage prohibition
Marriage imposes financial obligations (mahr and nafaqah) on the mukatab's dhimmah, which can divert him from earning to pay his kitabah (manumission contract). This is why permission is required: the master's interest is protected.
The text says 'لأنه يعيب نفسه؛ لما فيه من شغل ذمته بالمهر والنفقة'.
3
Understand the rationale for tasarrī prohibition
Tasarrī (taking a female slave as a concubine) requires full ownership (milk al-raqabah) over the slave. The mukatab does not have true ownership because he is still a slave; his ownership is incomplete. This prohibition is absolute.
The text says 'لأن مبناه على ملك الرقبة، ولا ملك له حقيقة؛ لوجود ما ينافيه وهو الرق'.
4
Note the exception and its effect
If the master grants permission for marriage, it becomes valid because the restriction is for the master's benefit. If the mukatab gains freedom before permission is given, the marriage becomes effective automatically.
The text says 'فإن أعتق المكاتب قبل إجازته نفذ ذلك النكاح على المكاتب'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا التزوج إلخ: يعني لا يملك التزوج بلا إذن؛ لأنه يعيب نفسه؛ لما فيه من شغل ذمته بالمهر والنفقة، ولم يطلق له إلا عقود توصله إلى تحصيل مقصوده، وهو عقد فيه اكتساب مال على ما بينا، ويملك التزوج بإذن المولى؛ لأن الحجر لأجله؛ لأن ملكه باقٍ فيه فجاز باتفاقهما؛ لثبوت ملكه في رقبته وفي "الخانية": المكاتب لا يملك وطء أمته، فإن وطئها ثم استحقت يؤاخذ المكاتب بعقرها في الحال تكملة البحر الرائق: ٩٤/٨] وإنما أطلق في عدم جواز التزوج؛ ليشمل التزوج بنفسه وبالتوكيل، فإن أعتق المكاتب قبل إجازته نفذ ذلك النكاح على المكاتب، هذا حكم التزوج، وأما التسري فلا يجوز ولو بإذن المولى؛ لأن مبناه على ملك الرقبة، ولا ملك له حقيقة؛ لوجود ما ينافيه وهو الرق. (تكملة، فتح)
Not marriage etc.: meaning he may not marry without permission, because it causes a defect to himself, due to the obligation of mahr and nafaqah on his dhimmah. He has been permitted only contracts that lead to achieving his purpose, which is a contract of earning money, as we explained. He may marry with the master's permission, because the restriction is for the master's sake, as his ownership remains in him, so it is permissible by agreement of both, due to the establishment of his ownership in his person. In al-Khaniyyah: The mukatab may not have intercourse with his slave girl; if he does have intercourse and then she is claimed, he is immediately held liable for her 'uqr (fair value). [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:94] The text is general in prohibiting marriage to include marriage in person and by proxy. If the mukatab frees himself before permission, that marriage becomes effective on the mukatab. This is the ruling of marriage. As for taking a concubine (tasarrī), it is not permissible even with the master's permission, because it is based on ownership of the person, and he has no real ownership due to the existence of its opposite, which is slavery. (Takmila, Fath)
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between marriage (nikah) and taking a concubine (tasarrī) for the mukatab. He may not marry without the master's permission because marriage imposes financial obligations (mahr, nafaqah) that divert him from earning for kitabah. With permission, it is permissible because the master's interest is protected. However, tasarrī (taking a slave girl as a concubine) is absolutely prohibited, even with permission, because it requires full ownership of the slave (milk al-raqabah), and the mukatab does not have true ownership due to his own slavery. The passage also notes that if the mukatab becomes free before the master's permission is granted, the marriage becomes effective.

Key Terms
تسري (tasarrī): Taking a female slave as a concubine for sexual relations.
عقر ('uqr): The fair value or compensation paid for intercourse with a slave girl if she is later claimed by someone else.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah (manumission) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible because permission removes the restriction.
B) Prohibited because the mukatab lacks full ownership of the slave.
C) Valid only if the slave is included in the kitabah contract.
D) Valid but requires payment of 'uqr immediately.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab may marry with his master's permission but cannot take a concubine under any circumstances due to his lack of full ownership.

Mukatab Purchasing His Wife with Her Child: Prohibition of Sale
Ruling on sale of wife bought with child by mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a wife bought by a mukatab with her child and explain the legal principle (tabi') that prevents her sale.

Core Idea
A mother follows her child's legal status; if the child is in kitabah, the mother cannot be sold.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in mukatab transactions: when a mukatab purchases his wife together with their child. The Hanafi school rules that he cannot sell the wife due to the principle of tabi' (dependency). Understanding this ruling clarifies how the child's entry into kitabah affects the mother's legal capacity, and contrasts with the case of a female mukatab buying her husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the key elements: a mukatab (a slave who has contracted manumission) buys his wife along with their child. Note that the child is already in kitabah (either born during kitabah or purchased).
2
State the ruling
It is not permissible (haram) for the mukatab to sell his wife under these conditions. The spouse remains a slave of the mukatab's owner, but the marriage continues.
3
Explain the legal basis: the child's entry into kitabah
The child is considered a dependent (mawlud) of the kitabah. Once the child is included in the kitabah, it cannot be sold. The mother, as a tabi' (follower), adopts the same prohibition because she is sold together with the child.
The principle 'al-tabi' tabi'' applies: the follower follows the principal.
4
Note the evidence: saying of Ali
The passage cites Ali's statement 'Her child frees her,' which indicates the mother's connection to the child in legal status.
This is a Qawl Sahabi, used as a supporting proof.
5
Compare with the female mukatab’s case
If a female mukatab buys her husband, she may sell him freely. The principle of juz'iyyah (child being part of the mother's kitabah) does not apply from her side, so the husband is not a tabi'.
This asymmetry highlights the gender-specific application of the tabi' principle in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يجز بيعها إلخ : يعني لو اشترى المكاتب زوجته مع ولده منها لم يجز له بيعها؛ لأن الولد لما دخل في الكتابة امتنع بيعه لما ذكرنا، فتتبعه أمه فامتنع بيعها؛ لأنها تبع له قال علي: "أعتقها ولدها" ولا تدخل في كتابته حتى لا تعتق بعتقه، ولم ينفسخ النكاح؛ لأنه لم يملكها، فجاز له أن يطأها بملك النكاح، وكذا المكاتبة إذا اشترت زوجها غير أن لها أن تبيعه كيفما كان؛ لأن الجزئية لم تثبت من جهتها تكملة البحر الرائق: ۹۷/۸]
It is not permissible to sell her etc.: meaning if the mukatab buys his wife along with her child from him, it is not permissible for him to sell her; because when the child entered the kitabah, its sale was prohibited as we mentioned, so the mother follows him, so her sale is prohibited; because she is a follower of him. Ali said: 'Her child frees her.' She does not enter his kitabah so she is not freed by his manumission, and the marriage is not annulled; because he did not own her, so it is permissible for him to have intercourse with her by right of marriage. Similarly, if the female mukatab buys her husband, except that she may sell him in any case; because partiality (juz'iyyah) is not established from her side. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/97)
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that if a mukatab purchases his wife together with their child, he may not sell the wife. The reason is that the child (having been born during kitabah or purchased) enters the kitabah as a subsidiary, and the mother follows the child as a 'tabi' (dependent). Since the child cannot be sold, the mother cannot be sold either. The wife does not herself enter the kitabah, so she does not become free upon the mukatab's eventual freedom. The marriage remains valid because the mukatab did not acquire ownership of his wife (which would annul the marriage), so he may continue to have intercourse with her by virtue of the marriage contract. Interestingly, if the scenario is reversed and a female mukatab buys her husband, she may sell him in all cases because the principle of 'juz'iyyah' (the child belonging to the mother) does not apply from her side—the husband is not her dependent in the same way.

Key Terms
تبع (tabi'): A dependent (in legal rulings), here the mother follows the child in the prohibition of sale.
جزئية (juz'iyyah): Partial ownership or relationship; here, the child being a part of the mother's kitabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible for a mukatab to sell his wife when he buys her together with their child?
A) The wife enters the kitabah and cannot be sold.
B) The wife follows the child who is already in kitabah.
C) The wife becomes free immediately.
D) The marriage is annulled.

Key Takeaway
The principle of tabi' in Hanafi fiqh prevents the sale of a mother when her child is in kitabah, while the reverse does not apply for a female mukatab.

Child's Kitabah After Mukatab's Invalid Marriage of Slaves
Ruling on child's kitabah after invalid marriage of slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a child born after a mukatab marries his slaves and makes kitabah for them enters the mother's kitabah, and explain why the mother is entitled to the child's earnings and blood money despite the invalid marriage.

Core Idea
Child follows mother in kitabah even if parents' marriage is fasid.

This passage from the later Hanafi commentary (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) addresses a nuanced scenario: a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) marries his male slave to his female slave, then makes kitabah for both. If a child is born, whose kitabah does it enter? The ruling is that the child enters the mother's kitabah, and her right extends to the child's earnings and blood money. The passage also resolves an objection about the validity of the marriage, showing how Hanafi fiqh preserves lineage even when the marriage contract is defective.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Identify the parties: a mukatab, his male slave, his female slave. The mukatab performs a marriage between the two slaves, then makes a kitabah contract with each slave. A child is born to the female slave. Ask: what is the child's status?
The marriage is invalid (fasid) because the mukatab lacks full authority over his slaves' marriage.
2
State the ruling
The child enters the mother's kitabah, not the father's. The child's earnings belong to the mother. If the child is killed, the blood money goes to the mother.
This is the conclusion from the passage.
3
Analyze the reasoning (dalil aqli)
The passage provides the rationale: the child follows the mother in legal attributes (al-ولد يتبع الأم في الأوصاف الحكمية). The child is considered a part (juz') of the mother, so the mother has stronger claim to the child's earnings and legal status.
This is an example of istihsan or qiyas based on the natural relationship.
4
Address the objection
An objection arises: the mukatab cannot validly give his male slave in marriage, so the marriage is void. How then can lineage be established? The answer: even a fasid (invalid) marriage can establish lineage in Hanafi fiqh, similar to the case of the mukatab's own child from his slave woman mentioned earlier.
This distinction between fasid and batil is crucial.
5
Apply to blood money
The passage explicitly mentions that the blood money goes to the mother, not the father. This follows from the same principle: the child is part of the mother.
This reinforces the mother's exclusive entitlement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زوج إلخ : يعني إن زوج المكاتب عبده من أمته، ثم كاتبهما ، فولدت الأمة دخل الولد في كتابتها، ويكون كسبه لها؛ لأن الولد يتبع الأم في الأوصاف الحكمية، فكان مكاتبا تبعا لها، فكانت أحق بكسبه من الأب؛ لأنه جزئها، فصار كنفسها ولو قتل هذا الولد تكون قيمته للأم دون الأب لما ذكرنا تكملة البحر الرائق: ٩٩/٨] لكن استشكل هذه الصورة بما تقدم من أ أن المكاتب لا يزوج عبده فكيف يثبت نسب الولد ويدخل في كتابة الأم؟ وأجيب بأنه لا منافاة؛ فإن تزويج المكاتب أمته من عبده ليس مفيدا لصحة عقده وملكه إياه، فالعقد غير صحيح، ذلك يثبت النسب كما في النكاح الفاسد، وكما مر في المسألة الأولى من ثبوت النسب لولد أمة المكاتب فتذكره، ومع وفيه كلام أكثر من هذا ذكره في "الفتح". (تكملة)
And if he marries etc.: meaning if the mukatab marries his male slave to his female slave, then makes a kitabah for them both, and the female slave gives birth, the child enters her kitabah, and its earnings are for her; because the child follows the mother in legal attributes, so it becomes a mukatab following her, so she is more entitled to its earnings than the father; because the child is a part of her, so it becomes like herself. And if this child is killed, its blood money goes to the mother, not the father, for what we mentioned. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/99) But this scenario is objected to based on what preceded: that the mukatab does not give his male slave in marriage, so how is the child's lineage established and it enters the mother's kitabah? And it is answered: There is no contradiction; for the mukatab's act of marrying his female slave to his male slave does not result in a valid contract because he does not own the right to give in marriage properly, so the marriage contract is invalid. Nevertheless, lineage is established as in a fasid (invalid) marriage, as was mentioned in the first issue regarding the establishment of lineage for the child of the mukatab's slave woman. And there is more discussion on this mentioned in al-Fath. (Takmila)
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a mukatab performs a marriage between his male slave and his female slave, then enters into a kitabah contract with both slaves simultaneously. If the female slave then gives birth, the child enters her kitabah (the mother's), and its earnings belong to her. The reasoning is that the child follows the mother in legal attributes; the child is considered a part (juz') of the mother, so she is more entitled to its earnings than the father. Likewise, blood money (diyah) for the child goes to the mother. An objection is raised: the mukatab does not have the authority to give his male slave in marriage (because the slave's marriage is invalid without master's consent, and the mukatab's own authority is limited). How then can the child's lineage be established? The answer is that even if the marriage is invalid (fasid), lineage is still established (like in an invalid marriage), and the child enters the mother's kitabah as was the case in the earlier ruling on the mukatab's own child from his slave woman.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An invalid marriage that nevertheless produces legal effects like lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The mukatab's kitabah.
D) The child does not enter any kitabah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a child born from an invalid marriage (fasid) still follows the mother for kitabah purposes, and the mother is entitled to the child's earnings and blood money.

Child of a Mukatab/Ma'dhun Who Married a Falsely Free Woman
Intra-Hanafi dispute on child's status following deceptive marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the child of a mukatab/ma'dhun who marries a woman claiming to be free, and apply the principle of ghurur to determine the child's status and the father's right of ransom.

Core Idea
The child's status depends on whether the father is treated as a free man due to deception — a clash between following the mother's status and protecting the deceived.

In this passage, the Hanafi school splits on a subtle issue: a mukatab or ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) marries a woman who claims to be free but is later proven a slave. The child's status — slave or free? The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold the child is slave. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (on his own) allows the father to ransom the child by paying its value, treating him as a deceived free man. This lesson dissects the proofs and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
Recognize that the dispute is between the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as one side, and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani as the other. Note the scenario: a mukatab or ma'dhun marries a free-claiming woman with master's permission, she gives birth, then is claimed as a slave.
The text uses 'عندهما' for Sahibayn and 'قال محمد' for Imam Muhammad.
2
State the Sahibayn's proof (dalil)
The Sahibayn argue that the child is born between two slaves (the father is a slave, the mother is proven a slave), so the child follows the mother in slavery. They reject ransom by value.
Dalil: the child follows the mother in status (al-walad li'l-firash wa li'l-umm fi al-riqq).
3
State Imam Muhammad's proof and qiyas
Imam Muhammad analogizes to a free man who is deceived (maghrur) by a slave woman's claim of freedom. In that case, the children are free upon payment of value. Here, the mukatab/ma'dhun is similarly deceived, so he may take the child by paying its value and later recover from the mother.
The qiyas relies on the principle of ghurur and consensus of Companions for the free case.
4
Compare the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the Sahibayn, the child remains a slave; under Imam Muhammad, the father can ransom the child and later get reimbursement from the mother after she becomes free.
The practical difference: possession of the child and financial liability.
5
Note the tarjih and authority
The text does not explicitly tarjih (prefer) one view. Later Hanafi fatwa may lean towards the Sahibayn as the majority, but some contexts may adopt Imam Muhammad's view.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah records both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكاتب أو مأذون إلخ يعني لو تزوج المكاتب أو عبد مأذون له في التجارة امرأة حرة بزعمها لا في الواقع بإذن المولى، فولدت المرأة، ثم استحقت بأن ادعى أحد أنها أمته فالولد عبد وليس له أن يأخذه بالقيمة عندهما، وقال محمد محله ولدها بالقيمة يعطيها للمستحق في الحال إذا كان التزوج بإذن المولى، وإن كان بغير إذنه يعطيها بعد العتق ثم يرجع هو بما ضمن من قيمة الولد على الأمة بعد العتق إذا كانت الفارة له؛ لأنه اعتمد على قولها، فصار مغرورا كالحر، فتكون أولاده أحرارا بالقيمة ولهما أنه مولود بين رقيقين، فيكون رقيقا؛ إذ الولد يتبع الأم في الرق والحرية، وتركنا هذا في الحر بإجماع الصحابة .. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٩٥/٢]
A mukatab or a ma'dhun etc.: meaning if a mukatab or a slave permitted to trade marries a free woman based on her claim (that she is free), not in reality, with the master's permission, and she gives birth, then she is claimed (as a slave) by someone claiming she is his slave woman, then the child is a slave. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), it is not for him to take the child by paying its value. Muhammad said: rather, he takes the child by paying its value, giving it to the claimant immediately if the marriage was with the master's permission; if without permission, he gives it after his manumission. Then he (the mukatab/ma'dhun) recovers from the slave woman (the mother) after she becomes free what he paid for the child's value, if the deception (ghurur) was from her; because he relied on her statement, so he becomes a deceived party (maghrur) like a free person, so his children are free by paying their value. Their argument (the other two) is that the child is born between two slaves, so it is a slave; for the child follows the mother in slavery and freedom. And we left this in the case of a free person by consensus of the Companions... [2/295 رمز الحقائق]
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of a child born to a mukatab or a ma'dhun slave (permitted to trade) who marries a woman claiming to be free, but who is later proven to be a slave. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), the child is a slave because it is born of two slaves (the father is a slave, the mother is a slave by claim), and the child follows the mother in status. Therefore, the father cannot take the child by paying its value. Muhammad al-Shaybani (it is implied he differs) holds a different view: the father (mukatab/ma'dhun) may take the child by paying its value to the claimant immediately if the marriage was with the master's permission; if without permission, he pays after he becomes free. Then he can recover that amount from the mother after she becomes free (if she is also freed later) because she deceived him by claiming to be free. This parallels the rule for a free man who marries a slave woman under deception—his children are free upon paying value. The consensus of the Companions is cited for the free person case, but the slave case is analogized with differences.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
غرور (ghurur): Deception; one who is deceived (maghrur) is entitled to recourse.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, to pay a sum for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child is a slave and the father cannot ransom him.
B) The child is free without payment.
C) The father may take the child by paying its value to the claimant.
D) The child becomes a mukatab like the father.

Key Takeaway
The child of a mukatab/ma'dhun who marries a falsely free woman is a slave according to the Sahibayn, but Imam Muhammad allows ransom, treating the father as a deceived free man.

Child Born to Mukatab's Slave-Girl: Entering the Kitabah
Ruling on child of mukatab's slave-girl entering kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a child born to a mukatab from his slave-girl enters the father's kitabah and explain the legal reasoning based on lineage and possession.

Core Idea
A child born to a mukatab from his slave-girl enters the father's kitabah; the child's earnings belong to the father.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the chapter on the mukatab: what happens when a mukatab has a child with his own slave-girl. The ruling establishes that the child enters the father's kitabah and the child's earnings belong to the mukatab. We will explore the underlying reasoning, an objection regarding the permissibility of intercourse, and the condition that restricts the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the birth of a child from a male mukatab and his slave-girl. The ruling: the child enters the kitabah and his earnings belong to the mukatab.
The ruling applies regardless of the permissibility of the intercourse.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is based on qiyas: lineage is established by the father's claim, so the child follows him in the kitabah; the child is in the possession of the mukatab, so his earnings belong to him.
The dalil is aqli (rational) rather than naqli.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection raised in al-'Inayah is that the mukatab does not own the right of concubinage (tamalluk al-sirri), so how can he have a child? The answer: 'he does not own' means it is not permitted, but if he does have intercourse and claims paternity, lineage is established.
This shows that legal effects can follow from prohibited acts.
4
Understand the restriction
The text restricts the ruling to the case where the child is born before the father's kitabah. This condition prevents the mother from becoming an umm walad, which would make her unsellable.
Some commentators interpret the condition differently; the key is that the mother's status as a sellable asset is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ولد له أمته ولد تكاتب عليه، وكسبه له. (ملتقط من الفتح والعيني وغيرهما) وإن ولد له إلخ: يعني إن ولد للمكاتب من أمته ولد دخل الولد في كتابته؛ لأنه بالدعوة تثبت النسب له، فيتبعه في الكتابة، ويكون كسب الولد للمكاتب؛ لأنه في حكم مملوكه، فكان كسبه له. وكذا لو ولدت المكاتبة ولدا دخل في كتابتها كما سنذكره. قال في العناية: واعترض عليه بأن المكاتب لا يملك التسري فمن أين له ولد الأمة حتى يدخل في الكتابة؟ وأجيب بأن معنى قولنا: 'لا يملك' لا يحل له وطء أمته ولكن إن وطئ وادعى النسب ثبت. وإنما قيدنا بكون الولادة قبل الكتابة؛ لأنها لو ولدت في ملكه لم يجز بيعها، سواء كان ولدها باقيا أو ميتا.
And if a child is born to his slave-girl, the child enters into his kitabah (contract), and the child's earnings belong to him. (Compiled from al-Fath, al-'Ayni, and others) 'And if a child is born to him etc.' means: if a child is born to the mukatab from his slave-girl, the child enters into his kitabah because through his claim of paternity, lineage is established for him, so the child follows him in the kitabah. And the child's earnings belong to the mukatab because the child is in the legal status of his slave, so his earnings belong to him. Similarly, if a female mukatab gives birth to a child, the child enters into her kitabah as we will mention. He said in al-'Inayah: An objection was raised that the mukatab does not own the right of concubinage, so how can he have a child from his slave-girl such that the child enters the kitabah? The answer is that the meaning of our statement 'he does not own' is that he is not permitted to have intercourse with his slave-girl, but if he does have intercourse and claims paternity, it is established. And we only restricted it to the child being born before the kitabah because if she gave birth while in his ownership, it would not be permissible to sell her, whether her child is alive or dead.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on the mukatab, establishing that if a mukatab has a child from his own slave-girl (even though he is not permitted to have intercourse with her), the child enters into the mukatab's kitabah contract and thus becomes a mukatab as well. The child's earnings belong to the mukatab. The commentator expands on this, explaining that the child follows the father in the contract due to established lineage. An objection from al-'Inayah is addressed: the mukatab does not have the legal right to concubinage (tamalluk al-sirri), but if intercourse occurs and he claims the child, the child's lineage is established. The ruling is restricted to childbirth before the kitabah; if the child is born after, the mother becomes an umm walad and cannot be sold.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
الكتابة (al-kitabah): The contract of manumission by payment; the child enters the same contract as the father.

Child of an Invalid Marriage Between Slaves Enters Mother's Kitabah
Ruling on child after invalid marriage and kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) still establishes lineage and state that the child enters the mother's kitabah.

Core Idea
Even an invalid marriage establishes lineage; the child follows the mother's kitabah.

This lesson examines a scenario where a mukatab (a slave in a kitabah contract) marries his own slave-girl to his own male slave, then enters both into kitabah. When a child is born from this union, the question arises: whose kitabah does the child enter? The Hanafi ruling resolves the tension between the invalid marriage and the legal effects of paternity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The mukatab marries his slave-girl to his male slave, then makes a kitabah contract for both. The child is born and enters the mother's kitabah; her earnings belong to her.
The child follows the mother in legal status.
2
Understand the objection
An objection is raised: the mukatab lacks authority to marry his slaves, so the contract is invalid. How can lineage be established or the child enter the mother's kitabah?
This objection challenges the ruling's coherence.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The response holds that the invalidity of the marriage does not prevent lineage or the legal consequences of intercourse. The child's status follows the mother, just as in a nikah fasid (invalid marriage) where lineage is still established.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the validity of the contract and its effects.
4
Compare to the previous case
The passage reminds us of the earlier ruling where a mukatab claims a child from his own slave-girl; similar logic applies: lineage is established despite the lack of a valid marriage.
Continuous reasoning across rulings.
5
State the practical consequence
The child is a mukatab following the mother's contract, and his earnings belong to her. If he is killed, blood money goes to the mother.
The ruling has implications for ownership and liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(Compiled from al-Fath, al-'Ayni, and others) From 'and if he marries etc.': meaning if the mukatab marries his male slave to his slave-girl, then makes a kitabah contract for both of them, and the slave-girl gives birth, the child enters her kitabah, and his earnings belong to her; because the child follows the mother in legal descriptions, so he becomes a mukatab following her, and she is more entitled to his earnings than the father; because he is a part of her, so he becomes like herself. But this scenario is problematic because of what was stated earlier that the mukatab does not marry his male slave, so how is the child's lineage established and how does he enter the mother's kitabah? Key Terms

Mukatab Marries Free Woman Under False Claim
Intra-Hanafi dispute on child ransom in a deceived marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on whether a mukatab can ransom his child born to a falsely free woman, and explain the two positions and their practical consequences.

Core Idea
A slave child born to two slaves remains a slave – but a deceived father may be treated like a free man.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mukatab or ma'dhun who marries a woman claiming to be free, only for her to be claimed as a slave after she gives birth. The child's status is clearly a slave, but can the father obtain the child by paying compensation? The two positions reflect different approaches to the principle of 'al-walad li al-firash' and the concept of a deceived husband (maghrur).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their legal status
The husband is a mukatab or ma'dhun (both are slaves with some capacity). The woman claims to be free but is later claimed by a third party as a slave. Their marriage is with the master's permission.
2
State the baseline ruling
The child is born a slave because the mother is a slave. This is undisputed. Note: the child follows the mother in slavery/freedom.
3
Present the first position (the two, Sahibayn)
They hold that the father cannot ransom the child. The child remains a slave because both parents are slaves at the time of birth; the father's slave status prevents him from being treated as a free person.
4
Present the second position (Muhammad)
He holds that the father can pay the value (qima) to the claimant and obtain the child's freedom, because the father was deceived (maghrur) by the woman's claim, making him like a free man in this regard.
5
Apply the details of Muhammad's view
If the marriage was with the master's permission, he pays immediately; if without permission, he pays after he becomes free (e.g., after his own kitaba ends). Then he can recover the amount from the woman after her freedom.

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mukatab or ma'dhun who marries a woman claiming to be free. After the birth, the woman is claimed as a slave. The ruling: the child is a slave. However, there is disagreement on whether the husband can obtain the child by paying the value (al-qima) to the claimant. According to the two (the Sahibayn – Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), he cannot; the child remains a slave. According to Imam Muhammad alone (or perhaps Abu Hanifa? the text is ambiguous), he can pay the value and obtain freedom for the child, with details depending on whether the marriage was with the master's permission. The commentator explains that the child follows the mother in slavery or freedom, and the exception for a free husband (where children are free by consensus) is set aside here. The dispute turns on whether the husband is considered deceived (maghrur) like a free man.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract to purchase his freedom from his master.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
المغرور (al-maghrur): The person who is deceived, especially in a contractual context.
القيمة (al-qima): The monetary value or compensation paid for the child to secure his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The child's status follows the mother, but a deceived father may be given a remedy, though the Sahibayn restrict it.

Kitabah of Umm Walad and Mudabbar
Ruling on kitabah of umm walad and mudabbar with sub-rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kitabah for umm walad and mudabbar, explain the umm walad's option between continuing and returning to slavery, and apply the consequences for death, inheritance, and subsequent children.

Core Idea
An umm walad under kitabah has a choice between immediate freedom with payment and deferred freedom as umm walad.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the unique situation of a slave woman who is both a mukatab (under kitabah) and an umm walad (mother of her master's child). It grants her a choice between two paths to freedom and outlines the legal consequences for lineage, earnings, inheritance, and her children. Understanding this ruling is essential for handling cases where a master enters a kitabah contract with his own umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the basic ruling: it is valid for a master to give kitabah to his umm walad or mudabbar, and if the umm walad gives birth during kitabah, she has a choice.
The ruling is found at the beginning and end of the passage.
2
Analyze the umm walad's option
She may either continue with the kitabah and pay her dues, or incapacitate herself and return to slavery, thus regaining umm walad status.
The reason: she faces two directions of freedom – immediate with payment (kitabah) and deferred without payment (umm walad).
3
Trace the consequences for lineage and 'uqr
Lineage of the child is established by the master's claim, regardless of the mother's choice. If she continues kitabah, she takes her 'uqr from the master; if the birth was pre-kitabah, no 'uqr.
'Uqr is the compensation for loss of virginity or for sexual access.
4
Examine death scenarios
If the master dies, she becomes free and kitabah debt is canceled. If she dies leaving enough wealth, the remainder goes to her child. If insolvent, no sa'y on the child.
The child is free (since umm walad's child is free), so no obligation to earn for the debt.
5
Handle subsequent children
If she gives birth to another child during kitabah, lineage is not established without a claim (due to prohibition of intercourse). But if she later incapacitates herself and gives birth, lineage is established unless explicitly denied. If she dies insolvent, that child (if not claimed) must earn (sa'y) to pay the kitabah debt because he is a mukatab following her.
This child's status as mukatab follows the mother's status.

Source Text

(ملتقط)
Section: On the kitabah of the mudabbar and umm walad and others. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/296] 'A female mukatab gives birth etc.' means: if a man makes a kitabah contract with his slave-girl and she gives birth to a child from him during the kitabah, she has a choice: if she wishes, she may continue with her kitabah and pay her due, and if she wishes, she may incapacitate herself from paying and return herself to slavery, becoming his umm walad; because she is faced with two directions of freedom: immediate with a price and deferred without price, so she may choose between them. The lineage of her child is established by his claim and does not require her verification because she is his property. If she continues with the kitabah, she takes her 'uqr from her master because she is more entitled to her earnings. This is if the child was from intercourse during the kitabah; if the birth was from intercourse before the kitabah, she has no 'uqr. If the master dies, she becomes free by istilad (having a child from him) and the kitabah debt is canceled. If she dies and leaves wealth to pay her kitabah, the remainder is inheritance for her child, because her freedom is established in the last part of her life. If she leaves no wealth, there is no sa'y (earning obligation) on the child because he is free. If she gives birth to another child, its lineage is not established without a claim, due to the prohibition of intercourse with her, until she incapacitates herself and then gives birth in a period possible for conception after her ta'jiz, then lineage is established without a claim unless he explicitly denies it, like other umm walads. And if he does not claim the second child, and she dies without paying, this child must earn (sa'y) to pay the kitabah debt because he is a mukatab following her. If the master dies after that, he becomes free and the sa'y obligation is canceled. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/296] (Fath) 'And if he gives kitabah to his umm walad etc.' means: it is valid for the master to give kitabah to his umm walad or mudabbar; because his ownership is established over each of them, even though the umm walad is not subject to valuation according to Imam Abu Hanifa, she still needs to obtain freedom before the master's death, and that is through kitabah, and there is no contradiction because she is faced with two directions of freedom. (Compiled)
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This section (fasl) addresses the kitabah of an umm walad (slave who bore her master's child) and a mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred upon master's death). The main ruling: if a man enters into a kitabah with his umm walad, she has the option during the kitabah to either continue with the kitabah (and pay) or to incapacitate herself and return to slavery, thus regaining her status as umm walad. This is because she has two paths to freedom: one immediate through the kitabah (with payment) and one deferred (through her child). The child's lineage is established by the master's claim. Various sub-rulings are given for scenarios of death, inheritance, 'uqr, and subsequent children. Additionally, it is valid to give kitabah to an umm walad or mudabbar because the master retains ownership over them, even though the umm walad is not subject to sale. The kitabah allows her to gain freedom before the master's death.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master and is thus free upon his death.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred to after the master's death.
السعاية (al-sa'y): The effort or earning required from a slave to pay off a debt or kitabah.
العقر (al-'uqr): Compensation paid to a female slave for loss of virginity or for sexual access, often due from the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt remains in force and must be paid by her heirs.
B) She becomes free and the debt is canceled.
C) She must continue paying to her master's heirs.
D) She is sold to pay the debt.

Key Takeaway
Kitabah for an umm walad is valid, granting her a choice between two paths to freedom, with specific consequences for lineage, 'uqr, and her children's status.

Options for the Mukatabah Who Gives Birth During Kitabah
States the choices for a female slave who becomes umm walad during kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to a mukatabah who gives birth during kitabah and identify the consequential rulings on lineage, 'aqr, inheritance, and sa'yah.

Core Idea
A mukatabah who bears her master's child can choose immediate freedom via kitabah or deferred freedom as umm walad.

When a female slave under a kitabah contract gives birth to her master's child, she faces a unique legal crossroads. The Hanafi school grants her a choice between two paths to freedom, each with distinct consequences for herself, her child, and the master. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the options and the ancillary rulings on lineage, compensation, and inheritance that follow from her decision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
The mukatabah may either continue her kitabah and pay the substitute to become free, or declare herself incapable and return to slavery, becoming an umm walad (who will be freed upon the master's death).
The choice is hers because two directions of freedom are presented: one immediate with payment, one deferred without payment.
2
Understand the ruling on lineage and 'aqr
The child's lineage is established by the master's claim; no confirmation from the slave-girl is needed. If she continues kitabah, she is entitled to the 'aqr (compensation) from the master because she earned it.
The 'aqr is only due if the child was conceived during the kitabah period, not before.
3
Distinguish birth during vs before kitabah
If the birth is from intercourse before the kitabah contract, she receives no 'aqr. The conception timing affects the ruling.
This distinction shows the importance of the contract period in determining rights.
4
Analyze the consequences of death
If the master dies, she becomes free as umm walad and the kitabah debt is cancelled. If she dies, her child inherits from her after paying the kitabah debt; if she leaves no property, the child owes nothing.
The child's freedom is protected even if the mother dies insolvent.
5
Determine the status of subsequent children
A second child born after she declares incapacity is automatically recognized as the master's unless he explicitly denies. If she dies without fulfilling kitabah, that child must earn (sa'yah) to pay the kitabah substitute, but if the master dies first, the child is freed.
The second child follows the mother's status as a mukatab child until the master dies.

Chapter: On the kitabah of the mudabbar and umm walad and others. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 296/2] 'A mukatabah gave birth etc.': That is, if a man made a kitabah contract with his slave-girl and she gave birth from him during the kitabah, she has the option: if she wishes, she may continue her kitabah and pay her substitute; if she wishes, she may declare herself incapable of paying the kitabah substitute and return herself to slavery, becoming his umm walad; because two directions of freedom have come to her: immediate with a substitute and deferred without a substitute, so she chooses between them. The lineage of her child is established by claim (da'wa), and her confirmation is not needed because she is his property in the slave-body. If she continues the kitabah, she takes her 'aqr from her master because she is more entitled to her earnings. This is if the child is from intercourse during the kitabah; if the birth was from intercourse before the kitabah, she has no 'aqr. If the master dies, she becomes free by istilad and the kitabah payment falls from her. If she dies and leaves property to pay her kitabah, what remains is inheritance for her child because her freedom is established in the last part of her life. If she leaves no property, there is no sa'y (earning) on the child because he is free. If she gives birth to another child, his lineage is not established without a claim because intercourse with her is forbidden to him until she declares herself incapable; if she gives birth thereafter within a period possible for conception after the incapacity, lineage is established without a claim unless he explicitly denies it, like other umm walads. If he does not claim the second child and she dies without fulfilling, this child must earn to pay the kitabah substitute because he is a mukatab following her; if the master dies thereafter, he becomes free and the sa'y is void. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 296/2]
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage details the situation of a female slave who enters kitabah and then gives birth to the master's child during the contract period. She is given a choice: either continue the kitabah and pay to become free, or declare inability to pay, return to slavery, and become an umm walad (mother of the master's child), which guarantees freedom upon the master's death. The reasoning is that she faces two paths to freedom: one immediate via payment (kitabah) and one deferred via becoming umm walad. The child's lineage is automatically established if claimed; no confirmation from her is needed. If she continues kitabah, she is entitled to the 'aqr (compensation) from the master because she earned it. The ruling distinguishes whether the child was conceived during kitabah or before. If the master dies during kitabah, she becomes free automatically as umm walad and the kitabah debt is cancelled. If she dies, the child inherits from her after deducting the kitabah debt; if no property, the child is free and owes nothing. A second child born after she declares incapacity but before the master's death is automatically recognized as his child, unless the master explicitly denies it. If the second child is not claimed and she dies before fulfilling kitabah, that child must work to pay the kitabah because he follows the mother's status as a mukatab child; but if the master dies first, the child is freed.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master, thereby acquiring special status: she becomes free upon the master's death.
المكاتبة (al-mukatabah): A female slave under kitabah contract.
السعاية (al-sa‘āyah): Earning or working to pay a debt; here, the child's obligation to work to pay the kitabah substitute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A mukatabah who gives birth during kitabah holds the option to choose between immediate manumission via payment or deferred freedom as umm walad, with various consequences for the child and inheritance.

Divisibility of Istilad in a Jointly Owned Mukataba
Intra-Hanafi dispute on istilad divisibility in a jointly owned mukataba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the divisibility of istilad in a jointly owned mukataba and derive the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) for compensation and lineage.

Core Idea
Istilad in a mukataba is divisible according to Abu Hanifa, but indivisible according to the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effects of istilad (a master fathering a child with his female slave) when the slave is a jointly owned mukataba (a slave under a kitaba contract). The key question: does istilad affect only the share of the owner who had the child, or the entire slave? Abu Hanifa and his two principal students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), disagree. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi methodology of analogical reasoning (qiyas) and the constraints of legal completion (takmil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the precise point of disagreement? The dispute centers on whether istilad is divisible when it occurs on a jointly owned mukataba. Determine the scenario: two owners jointly own a female slave, make her a mukataba, then one owner has a child by her, and later she becomes unable to pay the kitaba price.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that istilad is divisible in a mukataba, just as in a mudabbara. The kitaba contract prevents full ownership transfer, so istilad only affects the share of the owner who had the child. When the kitaba fails, that owner's share becomes umm walad, and he compensates the other owner.
His proof: Kitaba, like tadbir, prevents tamilk (transfer of ownership), so istilad cannot be completed over the other share.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn argue that istilad must be completed as much as possible. Since the kitaba can be annulled without harm, the entire slave becomes umm walad and the mukataba of the one who had the child. The prevention of kitaba is temporary, unlike tadbir which is permanent.
Their proof: Completion of istilad takes precedence; the kitaba can be set aside.
4
Analyze the compensation and lineage consequences
Under Abu Hanifa, the first owner pays half the slave's value and half the 'uqr; the second owner pays the child's value and the child becomes free. Under the Sahibayn, the first owner pays a compensation (half value as a mukataba per Abu Yusuf, or the lesser of half value or half remaining kitaba per Muhammad), and the second owner's child is not established in lineage nor freed.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
5
Determine the mufta bihi and memorize
The text instructs 'understand and memorize'suggesting Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position. Later Hanafi scholarship follows Abu Hanifa on the divisibility of istilad in a mukataba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمة بينهما كاتباها، فاستولدها أحدهما، فعجزت، فجعلت الكتابة كأن لم تكن، وتبين أن الأمة أم ولد للأول؛ لأن المقتضي للتكميل قائم والمانع من التكميل – وهو الكتابة – قد زال، يعمل المقتضي عمله من وجوده، فيضمن الأول للآخر نصف قيمتها؛ لأنه يتملك نصيبه لتكميل الاستيلاد، نصف عقرها؛ لوطئه جارية مشتركة، وضمن الآخر للأول قيمة الولد والولد حر بالقيمة؛ لأنه بمنزلة ولد المغرور؛ لأنه حين وطئها كان ملكه قائما ظاهرا، وولد المغرور ثابت النسب منه حرّ بالقيمة، والعقر كامل وطئه أمة الغير، هذا كله عند أبي حنيفة له. عندهما: لما لم يتجزأ استيلاد المكاتبة يصير هي أم ولد للأول، وهي مكاتبته كلها، وعليه نصف قيمتها حال كونها مكاتبة لشريكه عند أبي يوسف؛ لأنه تملك نصيب شريكه حال كتابتها وقيمة المكاتب على النصف من قيمة القن، وعند محمد: يجب عليه الأقل من نصف قيمته ومن نصف ما بقي من بدل الكتابة، ولا يثبت نسب ولد الأخير من الآخر، ولا يكون الولد حرا بالقيمة ويغرم العقر لها. فافهم واحفظ.
A female slave jointly owned by two, they both wrote a kitaba for her. Then one of them had a child by her (istilad). Then she became unable to pay, so the kitaba is considered as if it never existed, and it becomes clear that the female slave is an umm walad (mother of a child) for the first one, because the cause for completion (of istilad) is present and the preventer of completion, which is the kitaba, has ceased. The cause operates from its existence. So the first one guarantees to the other half of her value, because he takes ownership of his share to complete the istilad, and half of her 'uqr (compensation for intercourse) because he had intercourse with a jointly owned female slave. And the other (the second owner) guarantees to the first the value of the child, and the child is free by (paying) the value, because he is like the child of a deceived one (al-maghroor); because at the time he had intercourse, his ownership was apparent. The child of a deceived one has established lineage from him and is free by value. The 'uqr is complete because he had intercourse with a female slave of another. All this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), since istilad of a mukataba is not divisible, she becomes an umm walad for the first, and she is his mukataba entirely. And upon him (the first) is half of her value while she was a mukataba for his partner, according to Abu Yusuf, because he took ownership of his partner's share while she was in kitaba, and the value of a mukataba is half of the value of an ordinary slave. According to Muhammad, what is obligatory upon him is the lesser of half of her value or half of what remains of the kitaba price. And the lineage of the child of the second (the other owner) is not established from him, nor is the child free by value, and he pays the 'uqr for her. Understand and memorize.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage addresses a second dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, this time concerning a jointly owned female slave (mukataba) who is made mukataba by both owners, and then one owner has a child with her (istilad). The key issue is whether istilad is divisible when applied to a mukataba. Abu Hanifa holds that istilad is divisible in a mukataba (similar to a mudabbara) because kitaba prevents transfer of ownership, so istilad only affects the share of the one who had the child. Thus, upon her inability to pay, the kitaba is annulled, and she becomes an umm walad for that owner only, who must compensate the other owner for his share. The child born to the other owner (if he also had intercourse) is considered free by value and lineage is established. According to the Sahibayn, since istilad is indivisible in a mukataba, she becomes an umm walad entirely for the one who had the child, and she is also his mukataba entirely. They differ on the compensation: Abu Yusuf says he owes half her value as a mukataba; Muhammad says he owes the lesser of half her value or half the remaining kitaba price. The lineage of a child from the other owner is not established, and he must pay the 'uqr. The passage emphasizes the underlying principle of divisibility of istilad in a mukataba.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istīlād): The act of a master begetting a child on his female slave, which makes her a mother of his child (umm walad).
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master; she becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.
عقر (ʿuqr): Compensation paid for having intercourse with a female slave owned by someone else; it is similar to the dower (mahr) for a free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of a child born to the second owner (who did not have the first istilad) in the scenario of a jointly owned mukataba who fails to pay?
A) The child is a slave of the first owner.
B) The child is free by paying its value, and lineage is established from the second owner.
C) The child is not established and remains a slave.
D) The child is free immediately without payment.

Key Takeaway
Istilad on a jointly owned mukataba is divisible according to Abu Hanifa, leading to partial umm walad status and compensation for the other owner, while the Sahibayn hold it indivisible with different compensation rules.

Jointly-Owned Mukatibah: Paternity and Liability After Both Partners' Intercourse
Ruling on umm walad status and liability when both partners have intercourse with a jointly-owned mukatibah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on paternity and liability when both partners have intercourse with a jointly-owned mukatibah and identify the proof (istihsan and qiyas).

Core Idea
The first intercourse establishes paternity; the second partner's claim is invalid.

Imagine two partners jointly own a female slave who has a kitabah contract (she is a mukatibah, working to buy her freedom). Both partners have intercourse with her on separate occasions, each claiming paternity of the resulting child. Then she fails to pay her installments. Who is the father? What financial obligations arise? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, which relies on the principle of precedence and istihsan (juristic preference).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the facts: a jointly-owned mukatibah (owned equally by two partners), the first partner has intercourse, she gives birth and he claims the child. Then the second partner has intercourse, she gives birth and he claims. Finally, she fails to pay the kitabah (ajazat).
The sequence of intercourse and claims is critical.
2
Determine Paternity
According to the text, the child belongs to the first partner. The first intercourse is considered valid and establishes paternity because there was no prior claim. The second intercourse is analogous to zinā; the child is not attributed to the second partner.
This is based on istihsan: preserving the child's lineage to the first claimant.
3
Determine Liability
The first partner must pay his partner half the value of the slave and half the dower (ʿaqr) that would be due for intercourse with a free woman of her status. This compensates the partner for the loss of half the slave's value (since she becomes umm walad and cannot be sold) and for the dower that the partner could have claimed if he had intercourse himself.
The liability is wajib (obligatory).
4
Identify the Proof
The ruling is supported by istihsan and qiyas. The analogy is to a man who has intercourse with a jointly-owned slave (without kitabah): he also owes half the value to his partner. Here, the first partner effectively consumed the partner's share of the slave's value and enjoyment, so he must compensate.
All three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتابة العبد المشترك
كتاب المكاتب فوطئها أحدهما، فولدت فادّعاه ثم وطئ الآخر، فولدت، فادعاه، فعجزت، فهي أم ولد للأول، وضمن لشريكه نصف قيمتها ونصف عقرها، والولد للأول.
Book of the Jointly-Owned Slave's Kitabah. The book of the mukatib. So one of the two partners had intercourse with her, she gave birth and he claimed paternity, then the other had intercourse, she gave birth and he claimed paternity, then she became unable to pay (the kitabah). So she is an umm walad for the first, and he is liable to his partner for half her value and half her dower. And the child belongs to the first.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This is a ruling on a jointly-owned female slave who has been made a mukatibah (a slave who contracts to buy her freedom). The mas'alah: both partners have intercourse with her on separate occasions, each claiming paternity of the resulting child. Then she fails to pay her kitabah (ajazat). The ruling: she becomes umm walad of the first partner (the one who had intercourse first). The first partner owes his partner half the value of the slave and half the dower (mahr) that a woman of her status would receive. The child is attributed to the first partner. This is based on istihsan and the principle that the first intercourse establishes paternity and ownership. The second partner's intercourse is considered illicit; he owes full dower to the slave (or her representative). The text also implies that the second partner's paternity claim is invalid because the child already belongs to the first.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A female slave who has contracted with her master(s) to purchase her freedom by paying installments.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears her master's child and becomes free upon his death.
عقر ('aqr): The dower (mahr) due to a woman when a man has intercourse with her without a valid marriage; here, the compensation for intercourse with a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a jointly-owned mukatibah where both partners have intercourse and claim paternity before she fails to pay, who does the child belong to?
A) The first partner
B) The second partner
C) Both partners equally
D) The child is free

Key Takeaway
In a jointly-owned mukatibah, the partner who first has intercourse and claims paternity obtains the child and owes half the slave's value and half the dower to his partner.

Jointly-Owned Mukatibah: Tadbir Void Upon Failure to Pay
Ruling on tadbir becoming void and umm walad status for the first partner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a second partner of a jointly-owned mukatibah makes a tadbir but she fails to pay, and explain why the tadbir becomes void.

Core Idea
A tadbir by a partner on a jointly-owned mukatibah is void if the other partner's prior intercourse establishes umm walad status.

This lesson builds on the joint ownership of a mukatibah. Here we consider what happens when the second partner, instead of intercourse, makes a tadbir (deferred manumission). The ruling hinges on the precedence of the first partner's right of istilad, which retroactively shows the second never had full ownership. The tadbir becomes void, and the first partner compensates the second.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the tadbir (deferred manumission) made by the second co-owner. The ruling is that it becomes void (batil) upon the mukatibah's failure to pay.
2
Identify the cause of voidness
The cause is the first partner's prior intercourse, which established her as umm walad and gave him a superior ownership claim. The second partner's ownership was incomplete from the start, so his tadbir cannot stand.
The premise is that the first partner had intercourse before the tadbir.
3
Determine the consequences
The mukatibah becomes umm walad of the first partner. The first partner must compensate the second with half her value and half her dower (the marriage gift since she is a slave). The child belongs to the first.
Compensation covers the second's loss of share.
4
Identify the evidence
The ruling is based on ijma (consensus) of the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and qiyas on the case where a mukatibah becomes umm walad first; tadbir is secondary and invalid.
The dalil is aqli (rational) based on ownership conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن دبر الثاني ولم يطأها، فعجزت بطل التدبير وهي أم ولد للأول، وضمن لشريكه نصف قيمتها ونصف عقرها، والولد للأول.
And if the second partner made a tadbir (deferred manumission) and did not have intercourse with her, then she became unable to pay, the tadbir is void, and she is an umm walad for the first, and he is liable to his partner for half her value and half her dower, and the child belongs to the first.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This is a ruling on a variant of the previous case. Here, the second partner did not have intercourse but instead made a tadbir (postponed manumission after his death) on the mukatibah. Then she fails to pay. The ruling: the tadbir becomes void because the second partner did not have full ownership (the first partner's claim supervenes). She becomes umm walad of the first partner (the one who had intercourse earlier). The first partner owes half value and half dower to the second partner. The child belongs to the first. The reasoning: at the time of the first intercourse, the child was conceived and the first partner's right of istilad (procreation) became effective, which takes precedence over the tadbir. The tadbir is invalid because it requires full ownership or at least a share that can be effectively dedicated, but here the first partner's claim retrospectively shows that the second partner never had ownership of the whole. This is by consensus of the three (Imam, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A contract where a master declares that a slave will become free upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the second partner of a jointly-owned mukatibah makes a tadbir and then she fails to pay her kitabah, what happens to the tadbir?
A) It remains valid but she becomes umm walad of the first
B) It becomes void
C) It is valid and she is divided between the two
D) It is void and she is free

Key Takeaway
A tadbir by a partner on a jointly-owned mukatibah is void if the other partner's prior intercourse establishes umm walad status; compensation is due to the second partner.

Kitaba Survives the Master's Death
States that the kitaba contract remains valid upon the master's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of the master's death on the kitaba contract and explain the rationale using the concept of haqq al-'aqd and the analogies provided.

Core Idea
Kitaba survives the master's death because it is a contractual right, not a personal one.

When a master who has entered a kitaba contract with a slave dies, one might think the contract ends. However, Hanafi fiqh holds that the kitaba remains valid. In this lesson, we will examine the reasoning behind this ruling, focusing on the nature of 'right of the contract' and its persistence beyond death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that if the master dies, the kitaba is not invalidated (lam tansakh).
2
Analyze the First Reason
The ruling is because it is a 'right of the contract' (haqq al-'aqd). This means it is a contractual entitlement that persists beyond the parties' lifetimes. The passage gives analogies: tadbir, umm walad, debt, and the term in a sale when the claimant dies.
These analogies show that certain contractual rights survive death.
3
Analyze the Second Reason
The kitaba does not accept transfer to the ownership of the heirs. Instead, it remains under the ruling of the master's ownership, meaning the legal position of the master is continued by the heirs.
4
Synthesize the Implications
The mukatab continues his contractual relationship with the heirs, who stand in the master's place. The kitaba is not extinguished by death.
This ensures the mukatab's partial payment is not wasted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات السيد لم تنفسخ الكتابة؛ لأنها حق العقد، فلا تبطل بموت السيد كالتدبير وأم الولد والدين وكالأجل فيه إذا مات الطالب. ولأن الكتابة لا تقبل الانتقال إلى ملك الورثة، فتبقى على حكم ملك المولى. (تكملة البحر الرائق: 126/8)
And if the master dies, the kitaba is not invalidated, because it is a right of the contract, so it is not voided by the death of the master, like tadbir and umm walad and debt, and like the term in it when the claimant dies. And because the kitaba does not accept transfer to the ownership of the heirs, so it remains under the ruling of the master's ownership. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 126/8)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling statement with a rationale. It addresses the effect of the master's death on the kitaba contract. The ruling: the kitaba does not become invalid (ma tansakh). The reasons are twofold: First, the kitaba is a 'right of the contract' (haqq al-'aqd), meaning it is a contractual entitlement that survives the death of the master. Examples are given: tadbir (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) is not canceled by the master's death; umm walad (a slave-concubine who bore a child) has her status continue; a debt owed to the master is not extinguished; and the term (ajal) in a sale continues even if the buyer dies. Second, the kitaba does not transfer to the ownership of the heirs; rather, the mukatab remains under the ownership of the deceased master's legal estate. This means the kitaba continues as if the master were alive, with the heirs stepping in as successors. The passage is a memorization anchor: a simple, axiomatic rule.

Key Terms
حق العقد (haqq al-'aqd): A right that arises from the contract itself, persisting beyond the parties' lifetimes.
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration that a slave will be free upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of the kitaba contract upon the master's death?
A) It becomes void
B) It remains valid
C) The mukatab becomes free
D) The contract is transferred to the mukatab

Key Takeaway
The kitaba contract continues after the master's death because it creates a contractual right that persists, similar to debt or tadbir.

Coerced Marriage: Validity and Mahr Adjustment
Ruling on coercion in marriage: validity and mahr adjustment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a coerced marriage and identify the consequences for the mahr.

Core Idea
A coerced marriage is valid, but the mahr is capped at mahr al-mithl.

This lesson addresses a unique ruling in Hanafi fiqh: marriage under coercion is not automatically invalid. The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that the validity stands, but the mahr must be adjusted. Understanding this distinction helps avoid misconceptions that coerced contracts are always void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the passage concerns a man coerced into marriage. The Hanafi ruling is that the marriage is valid (sahih).
Coercion does not invalidate the contract in Hanafi law.
2
Analyze the mahr scenarios
If the specified mahr equals the proper dowry (mahr al-mithl), it is binding; if it exceeds, the excess is void.
The excess is void due to lack of consent to the extra amount.
3
Note the asymmetry for women
The text states 'nothing' for a coerced woman, meaning she owes nothing—likely because she is the one whose consent is lacking.
This brief mention requires further study from other texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لو أكره الرجل على النكاح . صح النكاح، فإن كان المسمى مهر المثل جاز، ولا يرجع على المكره بشيء، وإن كان أكثر من مهر المثل بطل الزيادة، لأنه فات الرضا بالزيادة بالإكراه، وإن أكره المرأة على النكاح، فلا شيء
Similarly, if a man is coerced into marriage, the marriage is valid. If the named dowry is the proper dowry (mahr al-mithl), it is permissible, and he cannot recover anything from the coercer. If it is more than the proper dowry, the excess is void because consent to the excess was lost due to coercion. If a woman is coerced into marriage, then nothing (is recovered).
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This is a matn statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the validity of marriage under coercion. The ruling: for a man coerced into marriage, the marriage is valid (sahih). Regarding the mahr, if the specified mahr equals the proper dowry (mahr al-mithl), it is binding and the husband cannot recover anything from the coercer. If the specified mahr exceeds the proper dowry, the excess portion is void (bāṭil) because the husband did not consent to that excess (his consent was overborne by coercion). He only has to pay the proper dowry. For a woman coerced into marriage, the text says 'nothing' – meaning she does not have to pay anything, presumably because she is the one whose consent is needed; but the exact meaning requires expansion. The overall principle is that coercion of the husband does not invalidate the marriage, but affects the mahr only to the extent of excess.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dowry, i.e., the standard dowry for a woman of similar status in her family and locality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the mahr in a coerced marriage exceeds mahr al-mithl?
A) The marriage is void.
B) The entire mahr is due.
C) The excess over mahr al-mithl is void.
D) The husband can recover all mahr from the coercer.

Key Takeaway
A coerced marriage is valid; only the excess mahr beyond the proper dowry is void.
